Divine Attributes

6 min
The Knowledge Analogy: One Attribute, Many Objects
Analogy with knowledge and accident to deny contradictory divine speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ash‘ari scholar uses the analogy of knowledge to show that a single divine attribute can relate to multiple objects, but cannot contain contradictory qualities like command and prohibition in the same act of speech.

Core Idea
One attribute can know many things, but cannot be both command and prohibition.

The Mu‘tazila argued that since divine speech commands some things and forbids others, it must be multiple. This passage counters using the analogy of knowledge: just as one knowledge covers all objects, one command could cover all actions. However, it then draws a crucial limit—a single thing cannot be inherently contradictory. Let's examine how this argument works.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Note the parallel structure between knowledge and command.
The passage uses a comparative argument (tamthīl).
2
Understand the knowledge analogy
The author says that knowledge of eternal and temporal is one quality that perceives all objects. Difference of objects does not necessitate different knowledges.
This is the core analogy.
3
Apply the analogy to command
Just as with knowledge, the relation of command to different commanded actions does not necessitate multiple commands. The author explicitly parallels 'difference of the commanded' with 'difference of the known'.
4
Identify the limit: contradictory speech
The author then distinguishes: speech that is both command and prohibition in itself would be two opposite descriptions for one thing, which is impossible. This is the key point against the Mu'tazila.
The analogy breaks at inherent contradiction.
5
Examine the accident analogue
The author likens the impossibility to the nature of accident: an accident cannot have a single essence that encompasses contradictory categories like knowledge, power, life, color.
This reinforces that a single attribute cannot have opposite essences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والعلم بالقديم والحادث علم بالشيء على ما هو به، وهو صفة صالحة لدرك ما يعرض عليه؛ سواء كان قديما أو حادثا، وجودا أو عدما، والذي يوازيه في تعلقه تعلق الأمر بمأمور معين وتعلقه بمأمور آخر فاختلاف المأمورين كاختلاف المعلومين ثم اختلاف المعلومين لا يستدعي اختلاف العلمين، كذلك اختلاف المأمورين لا يستدعي اختلاف الأمرين، لكن كلاما هو في نفسه أمر وهو في نفسه نهي اختلاف وصفين متضادين لشيء واحد وهو محال، ونظيره العرض في شموله أقسام الأعراض المختلفة الخواص؛ إذ يستحيل أن يكون للعرض ذات محققة على حيالها، وهي في ذواتها علم وقدرة وحياة ولون وكون.
And knowledge of the eternal and temporal is knowledge of a thing as it is, and it is a quality suitable for perceiving what is presented to it, whether eternal or temporal, existent or non-existent. And what parallels it in its relation is the relation of command to a particular commanded and its relation to another commanded. So difference of the commanded is like difference of the known. Then difference of the known does not necessitate difference of the two knowledges; likewise, difference of the commanded does not necessitate difference of the two commands. But speech that is in itself command and in itself prohibition—that is difference of two opposite descriptions for one thing, which is impossible. And its analogue is accident in its encompassing of different species of accidents; for it is impossible for accident to have a realized essence by itself, while they are in their essences knowledge, power, life, color, and being.
Source pages p. 285

Commentary
This passage is a response to the Mu'tazila objection. The author draws an analogy with knowledge: just as knowledge is one quality that can relate to both eternal and temporal objects without becoming multiple, similarly command as a single quality could relate to different commanded actions. However, the author distinguishes between relating to different objects and being inherently opposite. A single speech that is both command and prohibition in its essence would involve contradictory descriptions, which is impossible. The author then uses the example of accident: an accident like whiteness does not have a single essence that encompasses contradictory categories, but rather each accident is a specific essence. This undermines the idea of a single speech being both command and prohibition.

Key Terms
العلم
المتعلق
الأمر
النهي
العرض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The knowledge analogy proves that divine speech must be multiple because it commands and prohibits different things.
Correction: The analogy actually shows that one attribute can have many objects, so command could be single. But it also shows that a single speech cannot be inherently contradictory, so the Mu'tazila's conclusion is invalid.

Checkpoint
What does the author use the example of knowledge to illustrate?
A) Knowledge cannot comprehend opposites
B) One attribute can relate to many objects without being multiple
C) Command and prohibition are like objects of knowledge
D) Speech is not like knowledge

Key Takeaway
By analogy with knowledge, one attribute can have multiple objects, but contradictory attributes like command and prohibition cannot inhere in one speech.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever caught yourself saying one thing and meaning another, contradicting yourself? How does this lesson's emphasis on the impossibility of contradiction in God make you think about the consistency you owe to truth?
?
If God's speech cannot be both command and prohibition, what does that say about the clarity of His guidance? Are you living as if God's commands are clear and consistent, or do you make excuses for ignoring them?
?
The author uses an analogy from knowledge to show that one attribute can know many things. Do you trust that God's knowledge encompasses all aspects of your life, including those you hide? How would it change your daily habits if you truly believed that?

Refuting the Division-Description Distinction
Sanusi refutes the division-description distinction and the witness-absent transfer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author insists that command, prohibition, news, and inquiry are real divisions of speech, not mere descriptions, and how he refutes the Mu'tazila argument that divisions change between the witnessed and absent.

Core Idea
Divisions of speech are real and distinct; they cannot change between witnessed and absent.

To begin his refutation, the author tackles a key Mu'tazila claim that command and prohibition are not different divisions of speech but different descriptions of a single speech. He asserts they are real divisions, each with its own essence, and that 'speech' functions as a genus. He then dismisses the idea that what are divisions in the witnessed world become mere descriptions in the absent (God). Read on to see his emphatic rebuttal.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze. Then read the translation. Notice the author's forceful tone.
2
Identify the author's claim
The author directly rejects the claim that speech has different descriptions (awsaf) rather than divisions (aqsam). Find where he states this.
The contrasting terms are 'different descriptions' vs 'different divisions'.
3
Understand the genus analogy
The author says 'speech' is like a genus for its divisions. Think of animal as a genus for human, horse, etc. Each division is distinct by its own essence.
This strengthens the argument that divisions are real, not just descriptions.
4
Grasp the refutation of transfer
The author dismisses the Mu'tazila idea that divisions in the witnessed become descriptions in the absent. He asks: can realities change? He calls it nullifying realities.
This is a key a priori argument: intelligible categories are consistent across realms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقولكم: إن الكلام مختلف الأوصاف لا مختلف الأقسام غير صحيح، بل الأمر والنهي والخبر والاستخبار أقسام الكلام، والكلام منقسم إلى ذلك؛ إذ كل قسم ممتاز بخاصيته وحقيقته عن القسم الآخر، واسم الكلام كالجنس لها لا كالموصوف بها، ومن أمحل ما ذكرتموه قولكم: ما هو أقسام في الشاهد فهو أوصاف في الغائب، والحقائق كيف تتبدل والمعقولات كيف تتفاوت؟ وهل ذلك إلا رفع الحقائق وخرق الطرائق. وأما إلزام الحال فالجواب عنه أن المصحح للمختلفات المتضادات لشيء واحد غير وثبوت المختلفات المتضادات لشيء واحد غير، فإن الحياة مصححة للعلم والجهل والقدرة والعجز والإرادة والكراهة إلى غير ذلك من المعاني التي
And your saying: Speech is different descriptions, not different divisions—is not correct. Rather, command, prohibition, news, and inquiry are divisions of speech, and speech is divided into that; for each division is distinguished by its property and essence from the other division, and the name 'speech' is like the genus for them, not like that which is described by them. And among the most absurd of what you mentioned is your saying: 'What is divisions in the witnessed is descriptions in the absent.' How can realities change and intelligibles differ? Is that anything other than nullifying realities and breaking of methods? And as for the objection of the state, the answer to it is that the enabler for different opposites for one thing is other than the existence of different opposites for one thing. For life enables knowledge and ignorance, power and incapacity, will and aversion, and other meanings that
Source pages p. 285

Commentary
This passage is the author's refutation of a Mu'tazila distinction between 'descriptions' (awsaf) and 'divisions' (aqsam). The Mu'tazila had claimed that command and prohibition are descriptions of one speech, not divisions. The author insists they are real divisions, each with its own essence, and that speech is a genus. He then addresses an objection about the transfer of witnessed divisions to the absent (God). He argues that realities are consistent. Finally, he addresses the 'objection of the state' (ilzam al-hal), likely referring to the argument that opposites cannot inhere in one substrate. The author replies that the enabler (life) is different from the opposites themselves. Life is a single attribute that can be the basis for opposite qualities like knowledge and ignorance. Thus, a single divine speech could be the basis for command and prohibition if it had a role similar to life, but the text cuts off before completing the argument.

Key Terms
الأقسام
الأوصاف
الجنس
الشاهد والغائب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some Mu'tazila claimed that command and prohibition are not distinct divisions of speech but different descriptions of a single speech, and that what is a division in the witnessed world becomes a description in the absent (God).
Correction: The author argues that command and prohibition are real divisions with distinct essences, and that realities do not change between the witnessed and absent; such a view nullifies the very nature of realities.

Checkpoint
What does the author claim about command, prohibition, news, and inquiry?
A) They are different descriptions of speech
B) They are different divisions of speech
C) They are not part of speech
D) They are identical to each other

Key Takeaway
Command, prohibition, news, and inquiry are real divisions of speech; their reality does not change between the witnessed and the absent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In what area of your life have you subtly redefined a clear ethical command (like honesty or prayer) into a mere 'description' of your character—something flexible rather than a fixed duty? Will you restore it today as a non-negotiable division?
?
Sanusi asks, 'How can realities change and intelligibles differ?' When you treat God's standards as less binding on you than on others, are you not nullifying realities and breaking your own methods? What is one specific step you will take this week to align your actions with what you know is true?
?
The Mu'tazila claim may feel academic, but do you ever find yourself saying, 'That rule applies to others, not to me because I'm weak/unique/excused'? How does Sanusi's warning against 'breaking methods' challenge your exceptions, and what will you do to stop making them?

Life as Enabler for Opposites
Sanusi distinguishes enabler from enabled to handle the inherence of opposites.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author distinguishes the enabler (life) from the opposites it enables, thereby rebutting the objection about opposites inhering in one substrate.

Core Idea
The enabler of opposites is distinct from the opposites themselves; life enables but is not the opposites.

The author now addresses a specific objection: how can opposites like command and prohibition inhere in a single divine speech? The response distinguishes between the enabling condition (life) and the existence of opposites themselves. Using the example of life as a substrate for knowledge/ignorance, he shows that a single quality can be the basis for opposite attributes. Though the text cuts off, the key principle is clear.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on life as enabler
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the distinction between 'enabler' (musahhih) and 'existence' (thubut) of opposites.
2
Understand the key distinction
The author says the enabler for different opposites is other than the existence of the opposites. This is the core rebuttal.
The enabler is like a capacity; the opposites are actual instantiations.
3
Grasp the example of life
Life enables knowledge and ignorance, power and incapacity, etc. These are opposites, yet life is a single quality.
Life itself does not become the opposites; it only makes them possible.
4
Apply to speech
Although the text cuts off, the implication is that divine speech could similarly be a single enabler for opposite commands and prohibitions.
The author's argument parallels the life example to defend the unity of God's attribute of speech.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقولكم: إن الكلام مختلف الأوصاف لا مختلف الأقسام غير صحيح، بل الأمر والنهي والخبر والاستخبار أقسام الكلام، والكلام منقسم إلى ذلك؛ إذ كل قسم ممتاز بخاصيته وحقيقته عن القسم الآخر، واسم الكلام كالجنس لها لا كالموصوف بها، ومن أمحل ما ذكرتموه قولكم: ما هو أقسام في الشاهد فهو أوصاف في الغائب، والحقائق كيف تتبدل والمعقولات كيف تتفاوت؟ وهل ذلك إلا رفع الحقائق وخرق الطرائق. وأما إلزام الحال فالجواب عنه أن المصحح للمختلفات المتضادات لشيء واحد غير وثبوت المختلفات المتضادات لشيء واحد غير، فإن الحياة مصححة للعلم والجهل والقدرة والعجز والإرادة والكراهة إلى غير ذلك من المعاني التي
And your saying: Speech is different descriptions, not different divisions—is not correct. Rather, command, prohibition, news, and inquiry are divisions of speech, and speech is divided into that; for each division is distinguished by its property and essence from the other division, and the name 'speech' is like the genus for them, not like that which is described by them. And among the most absurd of what you mentioned is your saying: 'What is divisions in the witnessed is descriptions in the absent.' How can realities change and intelligibles differ? Is that anything other than nullifying realities and breaking of methods? And as for the objection of the state, the answer to it is that the enabler for different opposites for one thing is other than the existence of different opposites for one thing. For life enables knowledge and ignorance, power and incapacity, will and aversion, and other meanings that
Source pages p. 285

Commentary
This passage is the author's refutation of a Mu'tazila distinction between 'descriptions' (awsaf) and 'divisions' (aqsam). The Mu'tazila had claimed that command and prohibition are descriptions of one speech, not divisions. The author insists they are real divisions, each with its own essence, and that speech is a genus. He then addresses an objection about the transfer of witnessed divisions to the absent (God). He argues that realities are consistent. Finally, he addresses the 'objection of the state' (ilzam al-hal), likely referring to the argument that opposites cannot inhere in one substrate. The author replies that the enabler (life) is different from the opposites themselves. Life is a single attribute that can be the basis for opposite qualities like knowledge and ignorance. Thus, a single divine speech could be the basis for command and prohibition if it had a role similar to life, but the text cuts off before completing the argument.

Key Terms

المصحح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that if one thing enables opposites, it must itself be opposite or contain contradiction.
Correction: The author clarifies that the enabler (like life) is a single quality that does not itself become the opposites; it only makes them possible. There is no contradiction in a single enabler for multiple opposites.

Checkpoint
What is the example the author uses to show that a single thing can enable opposites?
A) Speech enabling command and prohibition
B) Life enabling knowledge and ignorance
C) Power enabling weakness
D) Will enabling aversion

Key Takeaway
The enabler of opposites (life) is distinct from the opposites themselves; this principle resolves the objection about opposites in one attribute.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you observe your own inner conflicts—such as the desire to obey God yet feeling laziness—do you recognize that your life (hayat) is not those conflicting desires but the enabler that makes them possible? How does this shift your responsibility to choose between them? This week, choose one specific act of obedience and perform it consciously as an act of using the life God gave you to follow His command.
?
If God’s life enables His speech to contain both command and prohibition, does this help you trust that His will is unified even when His commands seem difficult? Write down one area where you struggle to accept a divine command and pray that Allah grants you the conviction that His single will is perfectly wise.
?
The distinction between enabler and enabled means that you are not your temptations—they are enabled by your life, but you are more than them. How can you remind yourself daily that your identity is in being a servant of Allah, not in your fluctuating desires? Commit to a specific dhikr or dua that reinforces this truth every morning.

The Philosopher's Emanation Analogy and Its Refutation
Refutation of philosopher's emanation analogy using colored glasses example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a common emanation analogy fails to explain divine speech, and why the relation of speech differs from emanation.

Core Idea
Emanation analogy assumes diversity from the emanator; divine speech diversity is from relation, not the speaker.

The philosopher attempts to defend emanation from a single intellect by comparing it to sunlight passing through colored glasses. The author, however, rejects this analogy, showing that the source of diversity in emanation is the emanator, while in divine speech it is the relation. This lesson examines the analogy and the author's critique.

Guided Path
1
Read the philosopher's analogy
Read the passage carefully. Identify the philosopher's analogy of sunlight and colored glasses.
Notice the philosopher claims diversity comes from the emanator.
2
Understand the application to divine speech
The philosopher applies the analogy to divine speech, arguing that diversity in speech (command, prohibition) is due to relations.
This is a counterargument to the author's earlier objection.
3
Analyze the author's rejection
The author points out that in the analogy diversity is from the emanator (sun), while in speech it is from the relation. Thus the equation is unsound.
Focus on the phrase 'لم تستقم التسوية بين البابين'.
4
Reflect on the key difference
Consider why the source of diversity matters. The philosopher's analogy fails because it misattributes the origin of multiplicity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وربما يجيب المتفلسف عن إلزام الفيض المختلف عن العقل الواحد فيقول: أنا أقول: كما أن العقل واحد ففيض العقل أيضا واحد، لكن القوابل والحوامل تختلف فيختلف الفيض باختلاف المفيض؛ كالشمس إذا أشرقت على زجاجات مختلفة الألوان أعطت كل زجاجة لونا خاصا لائقًا بلونها القابل فتعدد الصور، واختلافها إنما حصل من جانب المفيض لا من جانب الفائض، وأنتم أثبتم كلاما واحدًا وهو في نفسه أمر ونهي وخبر واستخبار، فتعدد الخواص واختلافها فيه إنما حصل من جانب المتعلق لا من جانب المتعلق، فلم تستقم التسوية بين البابين بالتعلق والفيض. وأما كلامنا في المبدأ ووجوب الصفات له فأبعد في باب التنزيه عن الكثرة، وأعلى في منهاج التقديس عن اختلاف الخواص له، والصفات كلها راجعة إلى ذات واحدة هي واجبة الوجود بذاتها ووجودها مستند الموجودات كلها من غير أن يثبت له منها كمال أو يحدث له صفة، وهو تعالى بكمال جلاله عديم الاسم والصفة من حيث ذاته، وإنما يقال له: اسم وصفة من حيث آثاره فقط، وآثاره فاعليته الصادرة عنه لا معا بل على ترتيب الأول والثاني والراجعة إليه لا بغتة، بل على
Perhaps the philosopher answers the objection of the emanation differing from the single intellect, saying: I say that just as the intellect is one, so the emanation of the intellect is one, but the recipients and bearers differ, so the emanation differs according to the difference of the emanator; like the sun when it shines on glasses of different colors, it gives each glass a specific color suitable to its receptive color, so the forms multiply, and their difference occurs from the side of the emanator, not from the side of the emanated. And you have posited a single speech which is in itself command, prohibition, information, and inquiry; so the multiplicity of properties and their difference in it occurs only from the side of the relation, not from the side of the related. So the equation between the two chapters—through relation and emanation—is not sound. As for our speech about the Principle and the necessity of attributes for Him, it is further in the chapter of transcendence beyond multiplicity, and higher in the way of sanctification beyond the difference of properties for Him. All attributes revert to a single essence which is necessary of existence by itself, and its existence is the basis of all existents, without establishing for Him from them perfection or occurring for Him an attribute. He, exalted, in the perfection of His majesty, is devoid of name and attribute in terms of His essence; He is only called by name and attribute in terms of His effects only, and His effects are His activity issuing from Him, not simultaneously but in the order of first and second, and returning to Him not suddenly, but upon...
Source pages p. 286

Commentary
This passage contains two distinct moves. First, the author presents a philosopher's response to the objection that emanation (fayḍ) from a single intellect produces diverse effects. The philosopher uses the analogy of sunlight passing through different colored glasses: the sun's light is one, but the colors vary due to the receptacles. He then tries to apply this to divine speech: God's speech is one, but its properties (command, prohibition, etc.) differ due to the different relations to commanded things. The author rejects this analogy for the same reason given earlier: the relation of command to a commanded thing does not change the essence of speech; but here the philosopher turns the table, claiming the diversity comes from the side of the hearer, not the speaker. The author notes the equation is unsound because in emanation the diversity comes from the emanator (according to the philosopher), while in speech they claim it comes from the relation. Second, the author asserts his own view: regarding the First Principle (al-mabda') and the necessity of attributes, he is far more transcendent. Attributes all return to a single essence, which is necessary of existence. God in His essence is beyond names and attributes; names and attributes are only ascribed to Him from the perspective of His effects (āthār). His activities issue from Him not simultaneously but in an order, and return to Him not all at once. The passage ends incomplete, likely continuing the discussion of the order of emanation or creation.

Key Terms
فيض
متفلسف
عقل
مفيض
فائض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that emanation and divine speech are analogous in how diversity arises.
Correction: In emanation, the philosopher attributes diversity to the emanator (sun), while in divine speech it is from the relation of the speech to its object; the analogy is therefore invalid.

Checkpoint
What does the philosopher use as an analogy for emanation from a single intellect?
A) A single flame burning different materials.
B) Sunlight on different colored glasses.
C) A single sound echoed in different rooms.
D) A single seed growing into various plants.

Key Takeaway
The philosopher's emanation analogy fails because it misplaces the source of diversity, equating it incorrectly with divine speech.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The philosopher's analogy of colored glasses seems reasonable at first glance, but the author exposes its flaw. When was a time you accepted an appealing but flawed argument about Allah's attributes because it made sense to you intellectually? How does this passage challenge you to be more rigorous in your theology?
?
The author insists that diversity in divine speech comes from relation, not from any change in Allah. How should this understanding affect the way you read the Quran's commands and prohibitions? Do you treat them as temporary or as rooted in His unchanging will?
?
The colored glasses analogy attempts to explain multiplicity without change in the source. Reflect on a moment you tried to rationalize a belief about Allah in a way that compromised His transcendence. What steps can you take to ensure your understanding of Allah is based on revelation rather than human analogy?

Divine Attributes Revert to a Single Essence
Affirmation of divine transcendence: attributes return to essence, names from effects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's doctrine that all divine attributes return to a single essence, and that God is beyond names and attributes in His essence.

Core Idea
God's essence is beyond attributes; names refer only to His effects.

Having refuted the philosopher's emanation objection, the author now presents his own view on divine attributes. He emphasizes that all attributes revert to a single essence that is necessary of existence. God in His essence is above names and attributes; these are only ascribed to Him from the perspective of His effects (āthār). This lesson explores this transcendent conception of God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on attributes
Read the Arabic passage focusing on 'وأما كلامنا في المبدأ' to the end. Note the emphasis on transcendence (تنزيه).
The passage is incomplete, but the core doctrine is clear.
2
Identify the reversion of attributes
The author states that all attributes revert to a single essence that is necessary of existence.
This is a key statement: 'والصفات كلها راجعة إلى ذات واحدة'.
3
Understand the negation of names in essence
God in His essence is devoid of name and attribute; they are only from the perspective of His effects.
This is a high level of transcendence.
4
Reflect on the ordered issuance of effects
His effects (āthār) issue in order (first, second) and return to Him gradually.
The passage ends here, but the idea of order is important.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وربما يجيب المتفلسف عن إلزام الفيض المختلف عن العقل الواحد فيقول: أنا أقول: كما أن العقل واحد ففيض العقل أيضا واحد، لكن القوابل والحوامل تختلف فيختلف الفيض باختلاف المفيض؛ كالشمس إذا أشرقت على زجاجات مختلفة الألوان أعطت كل زجاجة لونا خاصا لائقًا بلونها القابل فتعدد الصور، واختلافها إنما حصل من جانب المفيض لا من جانب الفائض، وأنتم أثبتم كلاما واحدًا وهو في نفسه أمر ونهي وخبر واستخبار، فتعدد الخواص واختلافها فيه إنما حصل من جانب المتعلق لا من جانب المتعلق، فلم تستقم التسوية بين البابين بالتعلق والفيض. وأما كلامنا في المبدأ ووجوب الصفات له فأبعد في باب التنزيه عن الكثرة، وأعلى في منهاج التقديس عن اختلاف الخواص له، والصفات كلها راجعة إلى ذات واحدة هي واجبة الوجود بذاتها ووجودها مستند الموجودات كلها من غير أن يثبت له منها كمال أو يحدث له صفة، وهو تعالى بكمال جلاله عديم الاسم والصفة من حيث ذاته، وإنما يقال له: اسم وصفة من حيث آثاره فقط، وآثاره فاعليته الصادرة عنه لا معا بل على ترتيب الأول والثاني والراجعة إليه لا بغتة، بل على
Perhaps the philosopher answers the objection of the emanation differing from the single intellect, saying: I say that just as the intellect is one, so the emanation of the intellect is one, but the recipients and bearers differ, so the emanation differs according to the difference of the emanator; like the sun when it shines on glasses of different colors, it gives each glass a specific color suitable to its receptive color, so the forms multiply, and their difference occurs from the side of the emanator, not from the side of the emanated. And you have posited a single speech which is in itself command, prohibition, information, and inquiry; so the multiplicity of properties and their difference in it occurs only from the side of the relation, not from the side of the related. So the equation between the two chapters—through relation and emanation—is not sound. As for our speech about the Principle and the necessity of attributes for Him, it is further in the chapter of transcendence beyond multiplicity, and higher in the way of sanctification beyond the difference of properties for Him. All attributes revert to a single essence which is necessary of existence by itself, and its existence is the basis of all existents, without establishing for Him from them perfection or occurring for Him an attribute. He, exalted, in the perfection of His majesty, is devoid of name and attribute in terms of His essence; He is only called by name and attribute in terms of His effects only, and His effects are His activity issuing from Him, not simultaneously but in the order of first and second, and returning to Him not suddenly, but upon...
Source pages p. 286

Commentary
This passage contains two distinct moves. First, the author presents a philosopher's response to the objection that emanation (fayḍ) from a single intellect produces diverse effects. The philosopher uses the analogy of sunlight passing through different colored glasses: the sun's light is one, but the colors vary due to the receptacles. He then tries to apply this to divine speech: God's speech is one, but its properties (command, prohibition, etc.) differ due to the different relations to commanded things. The author rejects this analogy for the same reason given earlier: the relation of command to a commanded thing does not change the essence of speech; but here the philosopher turns the table, claiming the diversity comes from the side of the hearer, not the speaker. The author notes the equation is unsound because in emanation the diversity comes from the emanator (according to the philosopher), while in speech they claim it comes from the relation. Second, the author asserts his own view: regarding the First Principle (al-mabda') and the necessity of attributes, he is far more transcendent. Attributes all return to a single essence, which is necessary of existence. God in His essence is beyond names and attributes; names and attributes are only ascribed to Him from the perspective of His effects (āthār). His activities issue from Him not simultaneously but in an order, and return to Him not all at once. The passage ends incomplete, likely continuing the discussion of the order of emanation or creation.

Correction: The author teaches that all attributes revert to a single essence; God's essence is not multiple.

Checkpoint

B) They are only from the perspective of His effects.
C) They are eternal and separate from His essence.
D) They are metaphorical and have no real meaning.

Key Takeaway
God's essence is transcendent and beyond names; attributes are ascribed only through His effects.

?

?
The passage says attributes 'revert to a single essence.' That essence is necessary and the basis of all. What would it mean for your sense of self-worth if you truly believed that your existence is entirely dependent on that single essence, without any attribute accruing to Him from you? Will you live today with that dependence in mind?

Divine Attributes Without Multiplicity
The author affirms that God's names and attributes are derived only from His effects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author reconciles the affirmation of divine attributes with the absolute transcendence of God's essence.

Core Idea
Names and attributes describe God's effects, not His essence.

This passage tackles a central tension in theology: how can we speak of God's attributes without implying multiplicity or change in His essence? The author argues that attributes are real but only refer to God's actions and effects, not to His timeless, simple essence. Read on to see how this nuanced position safeguards divine transcendence while allowing meaningful discourse about God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Note the key terms: تنزيه (transcendence), تقديس (sanctification), أسماء (names), صفات (attributes), آثار (effects).
The passage is dense; read slowly.
2
Identify the core distinction
Locate where the author distinguishes between speaking about God from the perspective of His essence versus from the perspective of His effects. This is the key move in the argument.
The phrase 'من حيث ذاته' vs 'من حيث آثاره' is crucial.
3
Reflect on the implications
Ask yourself: Why does the author insist that no perfection accrues to God from His effects? How does this preserve transcendence? Connect this to the broader Islamic emphasis on tawhid and tanzih.
This step deepens understanding beyond memorization.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما كلامنا في المبدأ ووجوب الصفات له فأبعد في باب التنزيه عن الكثرة، وأعلى في منهاج التقديس عن اختلاف الخواص له، والصفات كلها راجعة إلى ذات واحدة هي واجبة الوجود بذاتها ووجودها مستند الموجودات كلها من غير أن يثبت له منها كمال أو يحدث له صفة، وهو تعالى بكمال جلاله عديم الاسم والصفة من حيث ذاته، وإنما يقال له : اسم وصفة من حيث آثاره فقط ، وآثاره فاعليته الصادرة عنه لا معا بل على ترتيب الأول والثاني والراجعة إليه لا بغتة، بل على تدريج الثاني والأول من غير أن يحدث كمال في ذاته لم يكن أو يستكمل من حادث كمال كان جل جلاله بذاته وتقدست أسماؤه لصفاته.
As for our discussion about the Principle and the necessity of attributes for Him, it is further removed in the chapter of transcendence from multiplicity, and higher in the path of sanctification from difference of properties. All attributes refer back to one essence that is necessary of existence by itself, and its existence is the support of all existents, without any perfection being established for it from them, or an attribute occurring for Him. He, exalted, in the perfection of His majesty, is devoid of name and attribute from the perspective of His essence. Rather, He is only called name and attribute from the perspective of His effects alone. His effects are His acts proceeding from Him not simultaneously but in an order of first and second, and returning to Him not suddenly but gradually from second to first, without any perfection occurring in His essence that was not there, or being completed from a contingent perfection. Glory to His essence and sanctified are His names for His attributes.
Source pages p. 286

Commentary
This passage is an exposition on the nature of divine attributes. The author states that when discussing the First Principle (المبدأ) and the necessity of attributes for Him, one must go further in transcendence (تنزيه) by avoiding any notion of multiplicity. All attributes refer back to a single essence that is necessary of existence and is the foundation of all existents. No perfection accrues to God from these attributes, nor does any new attribute arise in Him. In His essence, He is absolutely beyond names and attributes; names and attributes are only ascribed to Him from the perspective of His effects (آثاره), which are His acts. These acts proceed from Him in a sequential order, not all at once, and they return to Him gradually without implying any change or increase in perfection in His essence. This safeguards the divine transcendence and sanctification.

Correction: The passage does not deny divine attributes; it denies that attributes indicate multiplicity in the essence, and restricts the ascription of names and attributes to the perspective of God's effects. Attributes are real but do not imply composition or change in God.

Ash'ari Defense: Aspects Do Not Create Opposition in God's Speech
The Ash'ari argues that different relations do not entail opposition in a single attribute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ash'aris defend the unity of God's speech against the charge of multiplicity by distinguishing between opposition and difference in aspects.

Core Idea
Opposition requires total contrast; differing aspects do not break unity.

The philosophers argued that if God's speech contains multiple commands and prohibitions, it implies multiplicity in God's attribute of speech. In this passage, the Ash'aris respond by clarifying the nature of opposition (تضاد): true opposition requires complete contrariety in every respect, while differences in relations, times, aspects, or considerations do not create contradiction. They illustrate this with examples from ordinary language and the properties of a substance.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without over-analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the flow: the Ash'aris first state their position, then define opposition, then provide examples.
2
Understand the definition of opposition
Identify the key condition for opposition: two things must be opposite 'in every respect'. The passage stresses that this is a strict requirement.
Contrast this with mere difference.
3
Examine the examples
The passage gives several scenarios where commands and prohibitions coexist without opposition. Note how the relations (متعلقات) and aspects (وجوه) differ in each case.
Examples: commanding one thing and forbidding its opposite, or commanding one person and forbidding another.
4
Consider the substance analogy
The passage ends with the example of a substance (جوهر) having multiple attributes. This shows how one thing can have many descriptions from different aspects without contradiction.
Reflect on how this supports the Ash'ari argument.
5
Summarize the key insight
Synthesize what you've learned: The unity of God's speech is not threatened by diversity of commands because opposition requires total contrast, not just difference.
This is the core of the refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالت الأشعرية: نحن لا نثبت الحقائق المختلفة والخواص المتباينة لكلام واحد، إنما يلزمنا التضاد بين أمرين يتقابلان من كل وجه فيتضادان، فأما إذا لم يتقابلا بل اختلفت المتعلقات واختلفت الوجوه فلا يبعد اجتماعهما في حقيقة واحدة، ونحن لو قلنا الأمر بالشيء والنهي عن ضده أو الأمر بالشيء والنهي عن شيء آخر، أو أمر شخص واحد بشيء ونهي شخص آخر عن ذلك الشيء لا يستحيل، وكذلك إذا اختلف الزمان والمكان والوجه والاعتبار لم يحصل التضاد فلم يلزم الاستحالة، وهذا إن ألزم علينا التضاد، وإن ألزم علينا الاختلاف بين الحقيقتين دون التضاد فالجواب عنه أن اجتماع مثل ذلك في شيء واحد من وجوه مختلفة غير مستحيل، فإن الجوهر يوصف بأنه متحيز وقابل للأعراض وذو حجم ومساحة ونهاية، وهي صفات وأحوال ووجوه واعتبارات
The Ash'aris said: We do not posit different realities and diverse properties for one word of speech. Opposition only follows from two things that are opposite in every respect, and they then become mutually exclusive. But if they are not opposite, rather the relations differ and the aspects differ, then there is no impossibility in their coming together in a single reality. For if we say: commanding a thing and forbidding its opposite, or commanding a thing and forbidding another thing, or a single person commanding a thing and another person forbidding that thing, these are not impossible. Similarly, if time, place, aspect, and consideration differ, opposition does not occur, so impossibility does not follow. This is if opposition is alleged against us. But if difference between the two realities (without opposition) is alleged against us, then the answer is that the coming together of such (qualities) in a single thing from different aspects is not impossible. For the substance is described as being extended, receptive of accidents, possessing volume, area, and limit—these are attributes, states, aspects, and considerations.
Source pages p. 287

Commentary
This passage is the Ash'ari rejoinder to the philosopher's criticism about multiplicity in God's speech. The Ash'aris clarify that they do not posit different realities (حقائق مختلفة) or diverse properties for a single word of speech. They explain that opposition (تضاد) only occurs when two things are opposites in every respect. If the relations (متعلقات) and aspects (وجوه) differ, it is possible for them to be united in a single reality without contradiction. They provide examples: commanding something and forbidding its opposite, or commanding something and forbidding something else, or one person commanding and another forbidding the same thing—these are not impossible because the relations differ. Time, place, aspect, and consideration also remove opposition. Then they address the charge of mere difference (اختلاف) without opposition: even difference does not necessitate impossibility because one thing can have multiple aspects, just as a substance (جوهر) can be described with multiple attributes like extension, receptivity to accidents, volume, area, and limit—these are all different attributes, states, aspects, and considerations that do not contradict each other when viewed from different perspectives.

Key Terms
تضاد
متعلقات
وجوه
جوهر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the Ash'aris deny any multiplicity in God's speech, but they only deny different realities, not different aspects.
Correction: The Ash'aris affirm that one word of speech can have multiple commands and prohibitions when considered from different relations or aspects, without implying real multiplicity in God's attribute.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'aris, when does opposition (تضاد) occur?
A) When two things differ in any respect
B) When two things are opposite in every respect
C) When two things relate to different objects
D) When two things occur at different times

Key Takeaway
Opposition in attributes requires total contrariety in every respect; differing relations, times, aspects, or considerations do not create multiplicity in God's speech.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter what seems like a contradiction in Allah's attributes—such as His mercy and His severe punishment—do you trust that they are aspects of one perfect reality, or do you let the apparent tension shake your certainty? What aspect do you need to reconsider today to restore that trust?
?
The Ash'ari shows that even a physical substance can be described in many ways without conflict. In your own life, are there relationships or situations where you see only opposition, but perhaps with a shift in perspective you could see harmony? Will you ask Allah to grant you that insight before reacting?
?
If differing relations prevent opposition in Allah's speech, then your own limited understanding does not force contradiction upon Him. But are you willing to accept that your intellect cannot fully grasp how His command and prohibition coexist in His eternal word? What comfort or challenge does that humility bring you right now?

The Ash'ari Defense of the Unity of God's Speech
The Ash'aris defend the unity of God's speech against the charge of contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ash'aris argue that diverse modes of divine speech (command, prohibition, news) do not imply contradiction or multiplicity in God's essence.

Core Idea
Difference in aspect does not necessitate essential multiplicity.

The Ash'aris were accused of contradiction because they affirm that God's speech has many modes (command, prohibition, news). In this passage, they explain that true contradiction requires opposition in every respect, not just in some aspects. They use the example of a substance having multiple attributes to show that diverse descriptions can coexist in one reality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation to grasp the argument's flow.
Notice the author's style of presenting a claim, then a definition, then examples.
2
Identify the definition of true contradiction
The passage states that contradiction requires opposition in every aspect (من كل وجه). Understand that partial difference is not contradiction.
This is the key premise of the Ash'ari response.
3
Analyze the examples of commands and prohibitions
Look at the examples: (1) commanding a thing and forbidding its opposite, (2) commanding and forbidding different things, (3) commanding one person and forbidding another. Note how they differ in object or subject.
These show that differences in mutaʿalliqāt (objects of relation) or persons prevent contradiction.
4
Understand the analogy of the substance
The jawhar (substance) is described with multiple attributes (occupying space, receptive of accidents, volume). These are different considerations (iʿtibārāt) that do not conflict. Relate this to God's speech.
The analogy reinforces that multiple descriptions can apply to one essence without division.
5
Reflect on the distinction between divisions and attributes
The author refers to a prior distinction between aqsām (divisions) and awṣāf (attributes). This is a methodological foundation for the argument.
This distinction prevents conflating different categories.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالت الأشعرية: نحن لا نثبت الحقائق المختلفة والخواص المتباينة لكلام واحد، إنما يلزمنا التضاد بين أمرين يتقابلان من كل وجه فيتضادان، فأما إذا لم يتقابلا بل اختلفت المتعلقات واختلفت الوجوه فلا يبعد اجتماعهما في حقيقة واحدة، ونحن لو قلنا الأمر بالشيء والنهي عن ضده أو الأمر بالشيء والنهي عن شيء آخر، أو أمر شخص واحد بشيء ونهي شخص آخر عن ذلك الشيء لا يستحيل، وكذلك إذا اختلف الزمان والمكان والوجه والاعتبار لم يحصل التضاد فلم يلزم الاستحالة، وهذا إن ألزم علينا التضاد، وإن ألزم علينا الاختلاف بين الحقيقتين دون التضاد فالجواب عنه أن اجتماع مثل ذلك في شيء واحد من وجوه مختلفة غير مستحيل، فإن الجوهر يوصف بأنه متحيز وقابل للأعراض وذو حجم ومساحة ونهاية، وهي صفات وأحوال ووجوه واعتبارات مختلفة، وما استحال اجتماعها في حقيقة الجوهرية، وقد قدمنا الفرق بين الأقسام وبين الأوصاف.
The Ash'aris said: We do not affirm different realities and diverse properties for one speech. Rather, contradiction is necessary between two things that are opposed from every aspect and thus contradict each other. As for when they are not opposed but the objects of relation differ and the aspects differ, it is not unlikely that they coexist in one reality. For if we were to say: commanding a thing and forbidding its opposite, or commanding a thing and forbidding another thing, or commanding one person to do a thing and forbidding another person from that thing, this is not impossible. Similarly, if time, place, aspect, and consideration differ, contradiction does not occur, so impossibility is not required. If it is alleged that contradiction is necessary for us, or if it is alleged that there is difference between the two realities without contradiction, then the answer is that the coexistence of such [attributes] in one thing from different aspects is not impossible. For the substance is described as being occupying space, receptive of accidents, having volume, area, and limit, and these are attributes, states, aspects, and different considerations, and it is not impossible for them to coexist in the reality of substantiality. And we have already presented the difference between divisions and attributes.
Source pages p. 287

Commentary
This passage presents the Ash'ari argument defending the unity of God's speech (kalām) against the charge that its diverse modes—command, prohibition, information, etc.—would imply contradiction or multiplicity in the divine essence. The author first defines true contradiction (taḍādd) as requiring opposition in every respect. If two things are not opposed in every respect but differ in their objects of relation (mutaʿalliqāt) or aspects (wujūh), they can coexist in one reality. Examples illustrate this: commanding a thing and forbidding its opposite differ in object; commanding one person and forbidding another differ in subject. Even differences in time, place, or consideration prevent contradiction. The opponent might argue that at least difference (ikhtilāf) remains. The author responds by analogizing to a substance (jawhar), which has multiple attributes such as occupying space, being receptive of accidents, and having volume—all different considerations (iʿtibārāt) that coexist without contradiction. The phrase 'وَاعْتِبَارَاتٍ مختلفَة' emphasizes that these are distinct aspects of a single essence. The author concludes by referencing a prior distinction between divisions (aqsām) and attributes (awṣāf) as a methodological foundation.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that any difference in speech implies multiple realities in God.
Correction: The Ash'aris emphasize that difference in aspect does not necessitate essential multiplicity; true contradiction requires opposition in every respect.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'ari argument, when does true contradiction occur?
A) When two things differ in any aspect
B) When two things are opposed from every aspect
C) When two things have different objects
D) When two things exist at different times

Key Takeaway
True contradiction requires opposition in every aspect; diverse modes of God's speech coexist as distinct aspects of one divine speech.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the Quran and find verses that seem to command one thing and forbid another, does your heart become troubled by doubt about God's consistency? How does this lesson's teaching about differing aspects and objects help you trust in the perfect unity of God's speech right now? Commit to reciting one verse from each category today and reflecting on their different contexts.
?
Think of a relationship where your own words were misunderstood as contradictory because of different situations. How does the Ash'ari explanation of 'difference in aspects' reveal your need to more carefully understand God's speech before judging it? Will you seek tafsir before forming conclusions from now on?
?
The substance analogy shows that one thing can have many attributes without contradiction. In your own life, do you struggle to see how God can be both the All-Merciful and the Just Avenger? Which attribute do you tend to emphasize at the expense of the other? Repent of any imbalanced view and ask Allah to help you hold both in reverence.

One Speech, Many Modes: Ash'ari on Divine Speech
The Ash'aris contrast multiple temporal speech with one eternal divine speech encompassing all modes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ash'aris distinguish between temporal speech and God's eternal speech, and why divine speech can be one yet encompass all modes.

Core Idea
God's eternal speech is one attribute that substitutes for all modes without multiplicity.

The Ash'ari school faced the challenge of how one divine attribute can account for the diverse modes of speech we see in revelation. This passage from al-Ash'ari's creed resolves this by arguing that temporal speech is multiple due to its dependency on changing objects, while God's speech is a single eternal attribute that stands in for all modes. Let's explore this crucial distinction.

Guided Path
1
Classify temporal speech
Read the opening where the author notes that speech is divided into command, prohibition, information, etc., each distinguished by its most specific description.
This classification is based on witnessed (temporal) speech.
2
See why temporal speech is multiple
Understand that in the temporal realm, each mode is a distinct accident; command and prohibition of the same thing in the same aspect are contradictory. Multiplicity arises from the objects of relation.
The author stresses that the essence of temporal speech is multiple by the multiplicity of its aspects.
3
Consider the analogy with knowledge
Notice how the author compares speech to knowledge: different knowledges share the reality of knowledge but are distinguished by their objects. Similarly, speech divisions share 'speechness' but are distinguished by their specific descriptions.
This analogy prepares for the eternal case.
4
Grasp the eternal alternative
Read how eternal speech is specified by an eternal knowledge: it is a single attribute that substitutes for all modes without its essence becoming multiple or its properties differing.
This is the key Ash'ari doctrine: one eternal speech that functions as command, prohibition, etc., by substitution, not composition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقولهم: إن كون الكلام أمرًا ونهيًا وخبرا أقسام الكلام؛ إذ يقال: الكلام ينقسم إلى كذا أو إلى كذا وكل قسم إنما يتميز عن قسم بأخص وصفه في قول صحيح في الكلام شاهدًا، فإن الكلام القولي في اللسان أو النطق النفساني في الذهن من جملة الأعراض التي يستحيل بقاؤها، ولا تتحد ذاته وتتعدد جهاته بل ذاته متعددة بتعدد جهاته، فما هو أمر بشيء غير، وما هو نهي عن شيء غير، ويضاد الأمر نهي عن ذلك الشيء بعد اتحاد جهاته، فتعدد الكلام بتعدد المتعلقات، فصارت تلك الجهات أقسامًا، وانفصل كل قسم عن قسم بأخص وصف له بعد الاشتراك في حقيقة الكلامية؛ كالعلوم المختلفة إذا تعلقت بمعلومات مختلفة كانت بأخص وصفه بعد الاشتراك في قسمه أقسامًا مختلفة، وامتاز كل علم عن علم بأخص وصفه بعد الاشتراك في حقيقة العلمية. ثم إذا خصص الكلام بعلم قديم أو عالمية أزلية لم يلزم اتحاد الخصائص في ذات واحدة، بل العلم الأزلي في معنى علوم مختلفة نائبًا مناب الكل من غير أن تتكثر ذاته أو تختلف خواصه، وكذلك الكلام الأزلي في حكم الأمر والنهي والخبر والاستخبار نائبا مناب الكل من غير أن تتكثر ذاته أو تختلف خواصه، وهذا معنى.
And their saying: That speech is command, prohibition, and information are divisions of speech; for it is said: speech is divided into such-and-such, and each division is distinguished from another by its most specific description—this is a valid statement with respect to witnessed speech. For spoken speech on the tongue or the mental speech in the mind is among the accidents that cannot persist, and its essence is not one while its aspects are multiple, but rather its essence is multiple by the multiplicity of its aspects. So what is a command of a thing is other than what is a prohibition of a thing, and a prohibition contradicts a command of that same thing after unity of its aspects. Thus speech becomes multiple by the multiplicity of its objects of relation. So those aspects became divisions, and each division became separated from another by its most specific description after sharing in the reality of speechness. Like different knowledges: when they are related to different known things, they become different divisions by their most specific description after sharing in the category of knowledge, and each knowledge is distinguished from another by its most specific description after sharing in the reality of knowledge. Then, if speech is specified by an eternal knowledge or an eternal omniscience, the unity of properties in one essence is not necessary. Rather, the eternal knowledge is in the meaning of different knowledges, taking the place of all without its essence becoming multiple or its properties differing. Similarly, the eternal speech is in the ruling of command, prohibition, information, and inquiry, taking the place of all without its essence becoming multiple or its properties differing. And this is the meaning of.
Source pages p. 288

Commentary
This passage explains the crucial distinction between temporal (created) speech and God's eternal speech. The author begins by acknowledging that in human experience, speech is classified into command, prohibition, information, etc., each distinguished by its most specific description (a-khaṣṣ waṣf). In temporal speech—whether spoken or mental—the speech is an accident (ʿaraḍ) and does not persist; its essence is multiple because each mode is a distinct entity. For instance, a command and a prohibition of the same thing in the same aspect are contradictory. Thus multiplicity in temporal speech arises from the multiplicity of objects (mutaʿalliqāt). The author uses an analogy: different types of knowledge share the generic reality of knowledge (al-ʿilmiyya) but are differentiated by their objects, becoming distinct divisions. However, when we consider God's eternal attributes, the picture changes. God's eternal knowledge is one attribute that stands in for all possible knowledges (nāʾib manāba al-kull) without any multiplicity in its essence or properties. Similarly, God's eternal speech is one attribute that serves as command, prohibition, information, and inquiry all at once, without the essence becoming multiple or its properties differing. The passage concludes with 'وهذا معنى' (and this is the meaning of...), indicating that this is the intended understanding of the Ash'ari doctrine of the oneness of divine speech.

أمر

استخبار

أخص وصف
كلامية
علمية
متعلقات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that since human speech is multiple, God's speech must also be multiple (i.e., multiple divine attributes for command, prohibition, etc.).
Correction: The Ash'aris argue that God's speech is a single eternal attribute that functions as all modes by substitution (niyāba), not by composition or multiplicity.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how does the nature of temporal speech differ from eternal divine speech?
A) Temporal speech is one, eternal speech is many
B) Temporal speech is multiple in essence, eternal speech is one in essence but represents all modes
C) Both are multiple in essence
D) Both are one in essence

Key Takeaway
God's eternal speech is a single attribute that functions as command, prohibition, information, and inquiry without becoming multiple.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Which area of your life reveals that you do not truly believe in the unity of His speech—where you accept the command but ignore the prohibition, or vice versa? How will you change that today?
?
When you read the Qur'an, do you recognize that every verse—whether a legal ruling, a story, or a call to reflection—flows from the same single eternal speech of God? How does this realization challenge your habit of categorizing some verses as 'more important' than others? What would it mean for your daily recitation to treat each verse with equal reverence as coming from that one speech?
?
The Ash'aris teach that God's speech is not multiple like human speech—it is one that substitutes for all modes. Are there areas in your worship or ethics where you have assumed contradiction or confusion in God's commands (e.g., mercy vs. justice, hope vs. fear) because you were thinking of them as multiple separate speeches? How can you now embrace the unity of divine speech to resolve that tension in your heart?

Divisions of Witnessed Speech and Attributes of Divine Speech
The author distinguishes divisions of speech in the witnessed world from attributes in the unseen.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the divisions of witnessed speech (command, prohibition, etc.) relate to the attributes of divine speech, and why divine speech does not entail multiplicity in God's essence.

Core Idea
Witnessed speech divides by objects; divine speech unites all attributes without division.

In this passage, the author clarifies a critical distinction between the divisions of speech in the created world and the attributes of God's eternal speech. By analyzing how commanded, prohibited, and informative statements are distinct in human language, he shows that divine speech, being eternal and simple, encompasses all these functions without any multiplicity in the divine essence. This analogy helps prevent misunderstanding about God's attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text and its translation. Notice the key terms: divisions (aqsam), attributes (awsaf), witnessed (shahid), unseen (gha'ib).
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Understand the distinction between divisions and attributes
The author states that a distinction has already been made between divisions (aqsam) and attributes (awsaf). Divisions apply to created, multiple things; attributes apply to the divine essence.
This is a key conceptual difference.
3
Examine divisions in witnessed speech
In the witnessed world, speech is divided into categories like command, prohibition, and information. Each division is distinguished by its most specific property, and the multiplicity arises from the multiplicity of objects.
The author uses the analogy of sciences to clarify.
4
Apply the analogy to divine speech
For divine speech, which is eternal, it is not multiple but serves as a substitute for all divisions. Eternal knowledge or speech stands in for different sciences or speech acts without the essence multiplying.
This shows that God's attributes do not imply multiple essences.
5
Grasp the conclusion
The author concludes that the divisions in witnessed speech are like the attributes in unseen speech: in the unseen, the single essence has multiple attributes without division.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد قدمنا الفرق بين الأقسام وبين الأوصاف. وقولهم إن كون الكلام أمرا ونهيا وخبرا أقسام الكلام، إذ يقال الكلام ينقسم إلى كذا أو إلى كذا، وكل قسم إنما يتميز عن قسم بأخص وصفه في قول صحيح في الكلام شاهدا، فإن الكلام القولي في اللسان أو النطق النفساني في الذهن من جملة الأعراض التي يستحيل بقاؤها، ولا تتحد ذاته وتتعدد جهاته بل ذاته متعددة بتعدد جهاته، فما هو أمر بشيء غير، وما هو نهي عن شيء غير، ويضاد الأمر نهي عن ذلك الشيء بعد اتحاد جهاته، فتعدد الكلام بتعدد المتعلقات، فصارت تلك الجهات أقساما، وانفصل كل قسم عن قسم بأخص وصف له بعد الاشتراك في حقيقة الكلامية، كالعلوم المختلفة إذا تعلقت بمعلومات مختلفة كانت أقساما مختلفة، وامتاز كل علم عن علم بأخص وصفه بعد الاشتراك في حقيقة العلمية. ثم إذا خصص الكلام بعلم قديم أو عالمية أزلية لم يلزم اتحاد الخصائص في ذات واحدة، بل العلم الأزلي في معنى علوم مختلفة نائبا مناب الكل من غير أن تتكثر ذاته أو تختلف خواصه، وكذلك الكلام الأزلي في حكم الأمر والنهي والخبر والاستخبار نائبا مناب الكل من غير أن تتكثر ذاته أو تختلف خواصه، وهذا معنى قولنا الأقسام في الكلام الشاهد كالأوصاف في الكلام الغائب.
And we have already presented the distinction between divisions and attributes. And their saying: that speech being command, prohibition, and information are divisions of speech; for it is said: speech is divided into such-and-such or such-and-such, and each division is distinguished from another division only by its most specific description – a valid statement in witnessed speech. For spoken speech on the tongue or psychic utterance in the mind are among the accidents that cannot persist, and its essence does not become one while its aspects become multiple; rather its essence is multiple by the multiplicity of its aspects. So what is a command for something is other than what is a prohibition for something, and the command contradicts the prohibition regarding that same thing after the unification of its aspects. So speech becomes multiple by the multiplicity of the things related. So those aspects became divisions, and each division became separated from another division by its most specific description after sharing in the reality of speechness. Just as different sciences when they relate to different objects of knowledge become different divisions, and each science is distinguished from another science by its most specific description after sharing in the reality of scienceness. Then if speech is specified as eternal knowledge or eternal omniscience, it does not necessitate the unification of properties in a single essence; rather eternal knowledge in meaning is different sciences, serving as a substitute for all, without its essence multiplying or its properties differing. Likewise eternal speech is in the judgment of command, prohibition, information, and interrogation, serving as a substitute for all, without its essence multiplying or its properties differing. And this is the meaning of our saying: 'The divisions in witnessed speech are like the attributes in unseen speech.'
Source pages p. 288

Commentary
This passage defines the relationship between the divisions of speech in the witnessed world (shahid) and the attributes of divine speech in the unseen (gha'ib). The author begins by recalling a distinction already made between divisions (aqsam) and attributes (awṣaf). The opponents claim that speech being command, prohibition, and information constitutes divisions of speech. The author examines this in the witnessed realm: spoken or psychic speech is an accident that cannot persist, and its essence is not unitary while its aspects are multiple; rather its essence is multiple because its aspects are multiple. A command for something is different from a prohibition, and they are contradictory when regarding the same thing. Thus speech is multiple due to multiple objects (muta'allaqat). These aspects become divisions, each distinguished by a most specific property after sharing the common reality of 'speechness'. The author likens this to different sciences that share the reality of 'scienceness' but are distinguished by their specific objects. Then the author shifts to divine speech: if speech is characterized as eternal knowledge or eternal omniscience, there is no necessity that the properties become united in a single essence. Eternal knowledge stands in for different sciences without its essence multiplying. Similarly, eternal speech stands in for command, prohibition, information, and interrogation without its essence multiplying. The conclusion is that divisions in witnessed speech are analogous to attributes in unseen speech: in the witnessed world, multiplicity arises from the objects, while in the unseen, the single essence expresses multiple attributes without multiplicity.

Correction: The author argues that in divine speech, these are not divisions but attributes; the single eternal essence serves as a substitute for all of them without any multiplicity.

Key Takeaway
Divisions of witnessed speech are analogous to attributes of divine speech: the multiple aspects of created speech exist as non-multiple attributes in God's simple essence.

Divine Speech: One Meaning, Many Expressions
The author answers the objection that divine speech is one meaning expressed differently.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that divine speech is one meaning expressed differently, and how the author defends this view with Quranic examples.

Core Idea
Divine speech is one eternal meaning that takes different forms when expressed.

This lesson examines a key objection in the theology of divine attributes: if God's speech is eternal and one, how can the Quran contain commands, prohibitions, and narratives? The author presents the objection and then answers it by distinguishing between the eternal meaning and its temporal expressions. Let's see how the argument unfolds.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
The passage begins with an opponent's claim that divine speech is a single meaning (ma'na wahid) whose expression varies. Understand the categories: command, prohibition, past, future.
Notice that the objector's view is presented as a starting point, not the author's final position.
2
See the answer
The author agrees that speech is one meaning, but explains that the different forms are merely expressions based on aspects (i'tibarat). The eternal speech itself has no multiplicity.
This is the key move: distinguishing between essence and expression.
3
Examine the Quranic examples
The author gives examples from the Quran (Adam, Noah, Moses) to show how the same eternal meaning is expressed in different ways depending on the temporal context.
The example of Moses is incomplete in the text but illustrates the pattern.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم بم تنكرون على من يقول الكلام معنى واحد حقيقته الخبر عن المعلوم، ثم التعبير عن ذلك المعنى إذا كان المعلوم محكوما فعله بالأمر، أو محكوما تركه بالنهي، وإن كان في وقت قد وجد المعلوم وانقضى كان التعبير عنه عما كان، وإن كان في وقت لم يوجد المعلوم بعد وسيوجد كان التعبير عنه عما سيكون، فالأقسام المذكورة ترجع إلى التعبيرات بحسب الوجوه والاعتبارات، وإلا فالكلام الأزلي واحد لا كثرة فيه فاختلاف الخواص وتضاد المعاني، فكان قبل خلق آدم التعبير عن معلوم الخلافة في ثاني الحال إني جاعل في الأرض خليفة، وبعد إرسال نوح وانقراض عصره كان التعبير عن معلوم الرسالة في ماضي الحال إنا أرسلنا نوحا إلى قومه، وموسى قبل وجوده بالواد المقدس كان على صيغة الخبر، ولو عبر عن حال موسى قبل وجوده عما سيكون، وإذا عبر عن حاله وهو بالواد المقدس كان على صيغة الأمر:
Then why do you deny the one who says: speech is one meaning, its reality is information about the known thing, then the expression of that meaning – if the known thing is judged to be done, it is by command; or judged to be left, it is by prohibition; and if it is a time when the known thing already existed and passed, the expression about it is about what was; and if it is a time when the known thing does not yet exist and will exist, the expression about it is about what will be. So the aforementioned divisions refer back to expressions according to aspects and considerations; otherwise eternal speech is one with no multiplicity in it, so the differences of properties and contradiction of meanings [are only in expressions]. Thus before the creation of Adam, the expression about the known thing of caliphate in the second state was 'Indeed I am placing a caliph on the earth.' And after the sending of Noah and the passing of his era, the expression about the known thing of prophethood in the past state was 'Indeed We sent Noah to his people.' And Moses before his existence in the holy valley was in the form of information, and if he had expressed about the state of Moses before his existence, it would be about what will be; and when he expressed about his state while he was in the holy valley, it was in the form of command:
Source pages p. 289

Commentary
This passage presents and answers an objection. The opponent claims that divine speech is a single meaning (ma'na wahid) whose reality is information (khabar) about the known thing. The expression of that meaning varies: if the known thing is to be done, the expression is a command; if to be left, prohibition; if the known thing existed in the past, the expression is past tense; if it will exist in the future, the expression is future tense. Thus the divisions of speech (command, prohibition, information, etc.) are not real divisions in the essence of eternal speech but are merely different expressions (ta'birat) according to aspects and considerations. Eternal speech itself is one without multiplicity. The author supports this with examples from the Quran: before the creation of Adam, God expressed the future caliphate in the form of information ('I am placing a caliph'); after Noah's mission, He expressed it in the past tense ('We sent Noah'); and for Moses, before his existence, the expression was about the future, but when he was in the holy valley, it was in the form of command. This shows that the same eternal meaning takes different forms in expression without implying multiplicity in God's speech.

Key Terms
معنى واحد (ma'na wahid)
خبر (khabar)
تعبير (ta'bir)
اعتبارات (i'tibarat)
كلام أزلي (kalam azali)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the diversity of Quranic verses implies multiple divine speeches.
Correction: The passage shows that the diversity is only in expressions of one eternal meaning, not in the essence of divine speech.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The unity of divine speech is preserved by understanding that its various forms are expressions of one eternal meaning, not multiple speeches.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

One Speech, Many Expressions
Al-Shahrastani argues that variety of scriptural forms does not imply multiplicity in God's speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the variety of scriptural forms (command, prohibition, news) does not imply multiplicity in God's eternal speech according to al-Shahrastani's argument.

Core Idea
One eternal divine speech, but expression varies by time.

Al-Shahrastani tackles a key objection: if God's speech is one, why does the Quran contain commands, prohibitions, and news? He argues that these are merely temporal expressions of a single eternal reality, which is divine knowledge. This lesson walks through his reasoning and Quranic evidence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Notice the author's compact style and the flow of his argument.
The passage contains a sustained argument with Quranic examples.
2
Identify the three types of expression
Look for how al-Shahrastani categorizes expressions based on the temporal status of the 'known' (al-ma'lum): command, prohibition, and news (past/future). Understand the relationship between the eternal meaning and its temporal expression.
The expressions are relative to aspects and considerations, not differences in the essence.
3
Examine the Quranic examples and thought experiment
Study the examples of Adam, Noah, Moses, and the verse about Jesus. See how they illustrate the principle. Then consider the thought experiment about rational speech to grasp the timelessness of divine speech.
The Jesus verse (5:116) is the clinching evidence: God speaks of a future event in the past tense.
