قولنا: الأقسام في الكلام الشاهد كالأوصاف في الكلام الغائب، ثم بم تنكرون على من يقول: الكلام معنى واحد حقيقته الخبر عن المعلوم، ثم التعبير عن ذلك المعنى إذا كان المعلوم محكوما فعله بالأمر، أو محكوما تركه بالنهي، وإن كان في وقت قد وجد المعلوم وانقضى كان التعبير عنه عما كان، وإن كان في وقت لم يوجد المعلوم بعد وسيوجد كان التعبير عنه عما سيكون، فالأقسام المذكورة ترجع إلى التعبيرات بحسب الوجوه والاعتبارات، وإلا فالكلام الأزلي واحد لا كثرة فيه فاختلاف الخواص وتضاد المعاني، فكان قبل خلق آدم التعبير عن معلوم الخلافة في ثاني الحال: إني جَاعِلٌ فِي الْأَرْضِ خَلِيفَةٌ، وبعد إرسال نوح وانقراض عصره كان التعبير عن معلوم الرسالة في ماضي الحال: ( إنا أرسلنا نوحا إلى قومه)، موسى قبل وجوده بالواد المقدس كان على صيغة الخبر ولو عبر عن حال موسى قبل وجوده عما سيكون، وإذا عبر عن حاله وهو بالواد المقدس كان على صيغة الأمر: اخلع نعليك) فالاختلافات كلها راجعة إلى التعبيرات عن الكلام الذي هو المعلوم، حتى لو قدرنا لأنفسنا نطقا عقليًا سابقا على وجود المخاطب، باقيا على ممر الدهور كان المعبر عنه على حقيقة واحدة لا يتبدل والتعبيرات عنه على أقسام مختلفة لا تتماثل ولو قدرنا لنفوسنا نطقا عقليا مطابقا لإدراك عقلي عاليا على الدهر والزمان؛ بحيث نسبتهما إلى الماضي والحاضر والمستقبل نسبة واحدة، لم يشك أن الاختلاف لم يرجع إلى كثرة معان في ذاته، بل يرجع إلى ما يختلف بالأزمان، ألم تسمع الله تعالى وتقدس يخبر في كتابه الذي لا يأتيه الباطل من بين يديه ولا من خلفه حال عيسى عليه السلام عما سيكون في القيامة بقوله عز وجل: ( وَإِذْ قَالَ اللَّهُ يَا عِيسَى ابْنَ مَرْيَمَ أَأَنتَ قُلْتَ لِلنَّاسِ اتَّخِذُونِي وَأُمِّيَ إِلَٰهَيْنِ مِن دُونِ اللَّهِ قَالَ [المائدة : ١١٦]؟ أو ليس عبر في المستقبل بالماضي وبعد لم تقم القيامة، ولم يحشر الناس، ولم يحضر عيسى عليه السلام؟ ولكن لما كان القول الحق متعاليا ...
Our statement: The divisions in [phenomenal] speech are like the attributes in [divine] speech. Then, how do you reject the one who says: Speech is one meaning, its reality is information about the known thing. Then the expression of that meaning: if the known thing is such that its performance is commanded, then by command; or such that its abandonment is commanded, then by prohibition; and if the known thing has already occurred and passed, then the expression about it is about what was; and if the known thing has not yet occurred but will occur, then the expression about it is about what will be. So the mentioned divisions return to expressions according to aspects and considerations. Otherwise, eternal speech is one, having no multiplicity in it, so the differences of properties and opposition of meanings [in expressions] arise because before the creation of Adam, the expression about the known [matter] of the vicegerency in the second state was: 'Indeed I am placing on earth a vicegerent'; and after sending Noah and the extinction of his era, the expression about the known [matter] of the message in the past state was: 'Indeed We sent Noah to his people'; [concerning] Moses before his existence in the holy valley, it was in the form of news, and if He expressed the state of Moses before his existence, it would be about what will be; and when He expressed his state while he was in the holy valley, it was in the form of command: 'Take off your sandals'. So all differences return to expressions about speech which is the known [thing]. Even if we conceive for ourselves a rational speech prior to the existence of the addressee, remaining throughout the ages, the expressed [object] would be one reality that does not change, while the expressions of it are in different categories that are not alike. And if we conceive for ourselves a rational speech corresponding to a rational perception that is above time and space, such that its relation to past, present, and future is one relation, one would not doubt that the difference does not return to a multiplicity of meanings in its essence, but returns to what differs by times. Have you not heard God, exalted and sanctified, inform in His book, to which falsehood cannot come from before it or behind it, about the state of Jesus, peace be upon him, concerning what will be on the Day of Resurrection, by His saying, mighty and majestic: 'And when God said, O Jesus, son of Mary, did you say to the people, Take me and my mother as two gods besides God?' He said... [al-Ma'idah 5:116]. Did He not express the future in the past tense, while the resurrection had not yet occurred, nor the people gathered, nor Jesus present? But because the eternal speech ...
Source pages p. 289

Commentary
This passage is a sustained argument defending the unity of God's eternal speech against those who claim that the diversity of scriptural forms (command, prohibition, news, etc.) implies multiplicity in the divine essence. The author, al-Shahrastani, begins by stating his position: the divisions (aqsam) in phenomenal (shahid) speech are analogous to the attributes (awsaf) in divine (gha'ib) speech. He then challenges the opponent: 'How do you reject the one who says speech is a single meaning?' He explains that speech (at the divine level) is essentially information (khabar) about the known (al-ma'lum), i.e., the object of divine knowledge. The expression of that meaning varies depending on the temporal status of the known thing: if it is to be done, the expression is command; if to be avoided, prohibition; if already past, news about the past; if future, news about the future. So the apparent divisions are merely expressions relative to different aspects and considerations (wujuh wa i'tibarat). Otherwise, eternal (azali) speech is one, having no multiplicity. The differences in properties and seeming opposition of meanings arise only from the expressions. He then provides Quranic examples: before Adam was created, God expressed the future vicegerency as 'I am placing a vicegerent' (future expressed in present tense). After Noah, God referred to sending Noah as past news. Moses' future being commanded to remove his sandals was expressed as a command at the time. All differences are expressions about the same eternal speech, which is identical to divine knowledge. He then uses a thought experiment: if we conceive a rational speech (nutq 'aqli) existing before the addressee and persisting through ages, the object expressed (al-mu'bar 'anhu) remains one unchanging reality, while the expressions vary. Similarly, if we conceive a rational speech that is timeless, its relation to past, present, and future is uniform; then no one would doubt that the difference does not entail multiplicity of meanings in the essence, but only variation by times. Finally, he cites the verse where God speaks to Jesus on the Day of Resurrection using past tense ('when God said...') even though the event is future, proving that divine speech transcends temporal categories. The argument thus concludes that the diversity of scriptural forms is purely a function of temporal expression, not of any change or multiplicity in God's eternal speech.

Key Terms
الكلام (speech)
التعبير (expression)
المعلوم (the known/object of knowledge)
الأزلي (eternal)
الأمر (command)
النهي (prohibition)
الخبر (news/information)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The variety of scriptural forms (command, prohibition, news) implies that God's speech is multiple or changing.
Correction: Al-Shahrastani shows that these are merely temporal expressions of one eternal divine speech, which is essentially information about divine knowledge. The diversity arises from the temporal nature of the expressed events, not from any change in God's essence.

Checkpoint
What does al-Shahrastani argue is the reality of divine speech?
A) It is multiple and changes according to context.
B) It is one eternal meaning that is essentially information about the known.
C) It is identical to the Quranic text in Arabic.
D) It consists of commands and prohibitions only.

Key Takeaway
God's eternal speech is one, and the diversity of scriptural forms is due to the temporal nature of the expressed events, not to any multiplicity in God's essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you treat different parts of the Quran—commands, prohibitions, stories, promises—as if they come from a single, unified source, or do you sometimes feel they are disjointed? How would your relationship with the Quran change if you truly internalized that every verse is an expression of one eternal speech?
?
If God's speech is one and unchanging, then His command to you today is as real and immediate as His words to Moses or Noah. Are you living as if the Quran's commands apply to you at this very moment, or do you relegate them to history?
?
The lesson says that divine speech is 'above time and space,' yet you experience God's guidance in your own temporal moments. When you read a verse that seems to address a past situation, do you seek its timeless meaning for your life, or do you dismiss it as irrelevant? What would it take for you to hear every verse as if God is speaking it to you now?

The Oneness of Divine Speech and Diversity of Expressions
The author argues that differences are only in expressions, not in divine speech itself.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the diversity of expressions in divine speech does not imply any change or multiplicity in the essence of God's speech, and that divine speech is timeless and one.

Core Idea
Divine speech is one and timeless; all apparent differences are in expressions only.

This passage from al-Shahrastani's Nihayat al-Aqdam addresses a common theological confusion: if God's speech is one, why do scriptural expressions vary? The author argues that the differences are not in the speech itself but in the temporal expressions of it. He uses a thought experiment and a Quranic verse to illustrate that divine speech transcends time.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal English translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before interpreting.
Notice the flow from premise to example to conclusion.
2
Identify the main claim
Find the sentence that states the author's central thesis: 'All differences are only in the expressions of the speech which is the known.' This sets up the entire argument.
3
Understand the thought experiment
The author asks us to suppose an eternal mental utterance that remains through time. Recognise how this shows that the meaning is one while expressions differ.
The key point is that the utterance's relation to past, present, and future is identical.
4
Analyze the Quranic example
Read the verse about Jesus (5:116) and note that it uses the past tense for a future event (the Day of Resurrection). Ask yourself why this supports timeless speech.
The example demonstrates that divine speech is not bound by temporal sequence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالاختلافات كلها راجعة إلى التعبيرات عن الكلام الذي هو المعلوم، حتى لو قدرنا لأنفسنا نطقا عقليا سابقا على وجود المخاطب، باقيا على ممر الدهور كان المعبر عنه على حقيقة واحدة لا يتبدل والتعبيرات عنه على أقسام مختلفة لا تتماثل ولو قدرنا لنفوسنا نطقا عقليا مطابقا لإدراك عقلي عاليا على الدهر والزمان ؛ بحيث نسبتهما إلى الماضي والحاضر والمستقبل نسبة واحدة، لم يشك أن الاختلاف لم يرجع إلى كثرة معان في ذاته ، بل يرجع إلى ما يختلف بالأزمان، ألم تسمع الله تعالى وتقدس يخبر في كتابه الذي لا يأتيه الباطل من بين يديه ولا من خلفه حال عيسى عليه السلام عما سيكون في القيامة بقوله عز عن : ( وَإِذْ قَالَ اللهُ يَا عِيسَى ابْنَ مَرْيَمَ وَأَنتَ قُلْتَ لِلنَّاسِ اتَّخِذُونِي وَأُمِّيَ إِلَهَيْنِ مِن دُونِ اللَّهِ قَالَ )؟ أوليس عبر في المستقبل بالماضي وبعد لم تقم القيامة، ولم يحشر الناس، ولم يحضر عيسى عليه السلام؟ ولكن لما كان القول الحق متعاليا عن الزمان كان ما سيوجد كان قد وجد وكان نسبة الخطاب إليه وهو في ذلك الوقت كنسبته إليه وهو في هذا الوقت ومن أمكنه أن يرفع الزمان من صميم قلبه هان عليه إدراك المعاني العقلية، وسهل عليه معرفة تعلق العلم الأزلي بالمعلومات، والأمر الأزلي بالمأمورات، وعلم أن الاختلاف راجع إلى العبارات والتعبيرات
All differences are only in the expressions of the speech which is the known (al-ma'lum). Even if we suppose for ourselves a mental utterance (nutq 'aqli) prior to the existence of the addressee, remaining throughout the passage of ages, that which is expressed would be upon one reality not changing, and the expressions of it would be upon different categories not similar. And if we supposed for ourselves a mental utterance corresponding to a mental perception, high above time and duration, such that its relation to past, present, and future is one relation, one would not doubt that the difference did not return to multiplicity of meanings in its essence, but rather returns to what differs by times. Did you not hear that God, exalted and sanctified, informs in His Book, which falsehood cannot approach from before it or behind it, of the state of Jesus peace be upon him, concerning what will happen on the Day of Resurrection, by His saying, Exalted and Mighty: 'And when God said, O Jesus son of Mary, did you say to the people, Take me and my mother as two gods besides God?' He said... Or did He not express the future in the past tense, even though the Resurrection has not yet occurred, nor have people been gathered, nor has Jesus attended? But since the true speech (al-qawl al-haqq) is exalted above time, what will exist was already existing, and the relation of the address to it at that time is like its relation to it at this time. And whoever is able to remove time from the core of his heart, grasping intellectual meanings becomes easy for him, and knowing the attachment of eternal knowledge to known things, and the eternal command to commanded things becomes easy for him, and he knows that the difference goes back to phrases and expressions.
Source pages p. 290

Commentary
This passage is a demonstration that differences in divine speech are only in expressions, not in the essence. The author begins by stating the premise: all differences are due to expressions of the speech which is the known (al-ma'lum). He then supports it with a thought experiment: even if we imagine an eternal mental utterance (nutq 'aqli) that remains through time, its meaning would be one while expressions differ. Similarly, if we imagine a mental utterance matching a mental perception, being timeless, it would be clear that differences are not in its essence but in temporal factors. He then gives a Quranic example: the verse about Jesus (5:116) uses the past tense for a future event (the Day of Resurrection). This shows that divine speech, being exalted above time, can use past tense for future events because, in God's knowledge, what will exist already exists. The relation of the address to that event is the same regardless of time. He concludes that for one who can remove time from his heart (i.e., grasp timeless realities), understanding these intellectual meanings becomes easy, and he knows that differences are in phrases and expressions.

Correction: The passage argues that all differences are only in the expressions (al-ta'birat), not in the essence of divine speech, which is one and unchanging.

Checkpoint
What does the author's example of the Qur'anic verse about Jesus (5:116) illustrate?
A) That God speaks in different languages.
B) That divine speech can use the past tense for future events because it is timeless.
C) That the Qur'an contains contradictions.
D) That Jesus will be questioned on the Day of Judgment.

Key Takeaway
Divine speech is one and unchanging; diversity arises from temporal expressions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read a verse that describes a past event, do you recognize that God's speech is addressing you as directly now as it did those originally addressed? What would change in your daily engagement with the Qur'an if you truly internalized this timelessness?
?
The passage says that 'whoever is able to remove time from the core of his heart, grasping intellectual meanings becomes easy.' What temporal attachments—worries about the future, regrets about the past, or distractions of the present—are you unwilling to release, and how might that be preventing you from deeper understanding?
?
If you believed with certainty that God's speech is one and unchanging, and that all apparent differences in the Qur'an are merely expressions of that single truth, would you still pick and choose which verses to take 'personally'? How would your accountability before the whole Qur'an change?

Eternal Speech and the Illusion of Time
Author establishes that God's eternal speech relates uniformly to all times.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that God's eternal speech relates uniformly to all times, and that temporal differences are only in expression, not in the attribute.

Core Idea
Time is a veil; remove it, and the eternal speech is present every moment.

Before diving into the paradox of an eternal attribute appearing in time-bound forms, we must first grasp the principle that God's speech is not bound by time. The opening lines of this passage establish that relation, urging us to transcend temporal thinking.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening principle
Read the Arabic passage that begins with 'نهاية الإقدام في علم الكلام. عن الزمان كان ما سيوجد...'. It states that whatever will exist has already existed in God's knowledge and command.
Notice the phrase 'نسبة الخطاب إليه وهو في ذلك الوقت كنسبته إليه وهو في هذا الوقت' — the relation of address is the same.
2
Reflect on removing time
The author says whoever can remove time from his heart will understand how eternal attributes relate to temporal things. Reflect on why this is a prerequisite.
3
Note the conclusion
The passage concludes that differences are only in expressions and phrasings. This sets the foundation for the later analogy about garments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نهاية الإقدام في علم الكلام. عن الزمان كان ما سيوجد كان قد وجد وكان نسبة الخطاب إليه وهو في ذلك الوقت كنسبته إليه وهو في هذا الوقت. ومن أمكنه أن يرفع الزمان من صميم قلبه هان عليه إدراك المعاني العقلية، وسهل عليه معرفة تعلق العلم الأزلي بالمعلومات، والأمر الأزلي بالمأمورات، وعلم أن الاختلاف راجع إلى العبارات والتعبيرات. انظر كيف نشير إلى لمحات الحقائق على لسان الفريقين وإن كانا بمعزل عن دقائق الطريقين، وأنى لهم تصور نزول الروحانيات وتشخصها بالجسمانيات، كما أخبر التنزيل عنه: فَأَرْسَلْنَا إِلَيْهَا رُوحَنَا فَتَمَثَّلَ لَهَا بَشَرًا سَوِيًّا [مريم: 17]. وكيف يستقيم على مذهب المتكلم تمثل الروح بالشخص البشري: أبانعدم الروح ويوجد الشخص، وليس ذلك من التمثل في شيء، أو بأن يستعمل الروح شخصاً موجوداً بشرياً ولم يكن ذلك تمثلاً أيضاً، بل تناسخاً. وإذا لم يمكنه تقرير التمثل والتشكل؛ أعني تمثل الروحاني بالجسماني، كيف يمكنه تصور الأمر الأزلي يتمثل باللسان العربي تارة واللسان السرياني طوراً حتى يجب أن يقال: كلام الله كما يجب أن يقال: «هذا جبريل جاءكم ليعلمكم دينكم، ثم لباس جبريل يتبدل ولا تتبدل حقيقته التي هو بها جبريل، ولباس الكلام الأزلي يتبدل ولا تتبدل حقيقته التي هو بها كلام وأمر ونهي واحد أزلي. ﴿ وَإِنْ أَحَدٌ مِّنَ المُشْرِكِينَ اسْتَجَارَكَ فَأَجِرْهُ حَتَّى يَسْمَعَ كَلَامَ اللهِ ﴾ حق في حق المشرك المستجير، و ﴿ يَا مُوسَىٰ إِنِّي اصْطَفَيْتُكَ عَلَى النَّاسِ بِرِسَالَاتِي وَبِكَلَامِي ﴾ صدق في حق الكليم المستنير. فبين الكلامين فرق ما بين القدم والفرق وبين السمعين صرف ما بين الولاية والصرف. ولو كان الكلام في الموضعين حروفاً منظومة أصواتاً مقطعة بطل الاصطفاء على الناس وحصل التساوي واستماع الناس والخناس، ولكن حكم الكليم في خطابه يا موسى حكم الطريد في طرده يا فرعون، ولكان الذي لا يسمع من موسى أسعد حالاً من موسى؛ إذ سمع من الشجرة التي هي جماد، وفي الموضعين الكلام حروف وأصوات.
The End of the Stride in the Science of Kalam. About time: whatever will exist has already existed, and the relation of address to Him at that time is like His relation to Him at this time. Whoever can remove time from the core of his heart finds it easy to perceive intellectual meanings, and easy to know the connection of eternal knowledge to known things, and the eternal command to commanded things, and knows that difference refers back to expressions and phrasings. Look how we point to flashes of truths in the language of the two groups, though they are removed from the subtleties of the two paths. How can they conceive the descent of spiritual beings and their embodiment in physical forms, as the Revelation informed about it: 'Then We sent to her Our Spirit, and it appeared to her as a well-proportioned man' [Maryam: 17]. How can the theologian's doctrine accommodate the spirit's manifestation in a human person: either the spirit is annihilated and the person exists—that is not manifestation at all—or the spirit uses an already existing human person and that is not manifestation either, but rather transmigration. If he cannot establish manifestation and embodiment—meaning the spiritual manifesting in physical—how can he conceive the eternal command manifesting in the Arabic tongue one time and the Syriac tongue another time, so that it must be said: 'the speech of God' just as it must be said: 'This is Jibril coming to teach you your religion; then Jibril's garment changes but his essence, by which he is Jibril, does not change; and the garment of eternal speech changes but its essence, by which it is speech and command and prohibition, one eternal, does not change.' 'And if any one of the polytheists seeks your protection, then grant him protection so that he may hear the speech of Allah' is true regarding the polytheist who seeks protection, and 'O Moses, indeed I have chosen you over the people with My messages and My speech' is true regarding the intimate interlocutor enlightened. So between the two speeches is the difference between pre-eternity and createdness, and between the two hearings is the distinction between sainthood and degradation. And if speech in both places was organized letters and segmented sounds, the election over the people would be invalidated, equality would occur, and the hearing of the people and the devil would be the same. But the state of the intimate interlocutor in his address 'O Moses' is like the state of the outcast in his expulsion 'O Pharaoh'; and the one who does not hear from Moses would be in a better state than Moses, since he heard from the tree, which is inanimate. And in both places speech is letters and sounds.
Source pages p. 291

Commentary
This passage is a complex theological argument about the nature of God's eternal speech (الكلام الأزلي) and its manifestation in created sounds and letters. The author begins by establishing a principle: the relation of God's address to any time is uniform—past, present, future—because God's knowledge and command are eternal, not subject to change. Whoever grasps this can understand how eternal attributes relate to temporal things without alteration. He then introduces the problem of how spiritual realities (الروحانيات) can manifest in physical forms (الجسمانيات), using the Quranic example of the angel Gabriel appearing to Mary as a human (Q19:17). He challenges the theologian (المتكلم) to explain this: if the spirit (the angel) is annihilated and a person is created anew, that is not manifestation but substitution; if the spirit uses an existing human body, that is not manifestation but transmigration (تناسخ). Since the theologian cannot properly account for angelic manifestation, how can he conceive of the eternal command (الأمر الأزلي) manifesting in different human languages (Arabic, Syriac) without change? The author then introduces an analogy: just as the angel Jibril appears in different forms (clothing) while his essence remains unchanged, so the eternal speech 'wears' different garment of sounds and words while remaining one eternal essence. He cites two Quranic verses: one about a polytheist hearing 'the speech of Allah' (Q9:6) and one about Moses being addressed directly by God (Q7:144). Both instances involve sounds and letters, yet the author insists there is a fundamental difference: the first is a created temporal speech, while the second is a manifestation of the eternal speech. If both were merely created sounds, there would be no special distinction for Moses. In fact, the polytheist would be equal to Moses, and even an inanimate tree would be superior to Moses because it 'spoke' to him. The author then makes a striking concession: 'And in both places speech is letters and sounds' (وفي الموضعين الكلام حروف وأصوات). This seems to acknowledge that the phenomenal form is the same, but the ontological reality differs—one is created, one is eternal. The passage emphasizes the Ash'ari doctrine that God's speech is one eternal attribute, which expresses itself in temporal sounds without division or change.

الأمر الأزلي
العبارات

God's eternal speech relates identically to all times; temporal differences are merely in the expressions, not in the divine attribute itself.

Manifestation of the Eternal: From Gabriel's Garments to Divine Speech
Author argues that eternal speech manifests in temporal forms like Jibril's garments, using Quranic examples and analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how eternal speech manifests in temporal forms without change, using the analogy of Gabriel's garments.

Core Idea
Eternal speech wears time like a garment; its essence remains one.

Having established that God's speech is eternal and relates uniformly to time, we now face a paradox: how can an unchanging attribute appear as specific sounds and words? The author tackles this by first examining the problem of spiritual beings manifesting physically, then resolving it with a profound analogy.

Guided Path
1
Read the problem of manifestation
Read from 'انظر كيف نشير إلى لمحات الحقائق' to 'بل تناسخاً'. See how the author challenges the theologian to explain Gabriel's appearance as a human.
Two failed explanations are given: annihilation of the spirit or transmigration.
2
Understand the analogy of Jibril's garments
Read the analogy: Jibril's garment changes but his essence does not. This is applied to eternal speech: its outward forms change, but its essence remains one.
3
Analyze the Quranic examples
Consider the two verses: one about a polytheist hearing 'the speech of Allah' and Moses being addressed. Notice the author's claim that both involve sounds and letters, yet they differ ontologically.
4
Reflect on the final concession
The author ends with 'وفي الموضعين الكلام حروف وأصوات'. This shows that the phenomenal form is identical, but the reality differs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نهاية الإقدام في علم الكلام. عن الزمان كان ما سيوجد كان قد وجد وكان نسبة الخطاب إليه وهو في ذلك الوقت كنسبته إليه وهو في هذا الوقت. ومن أمكنه أن يرفع الزمان من صميم قلبه هان عليه إدراك المعاني العقلية، وسهل عليه معرفة تعلق العلم الأزلي بالمعلومات، والأمر الأزلي بالمأمورات، وعلم أن الاختلاف راجع إلى العبارات والتعبيرات. انظر كيف نشير إلى لمحات الحقائق على لسان الفريقين وإن كانا بمعزل عن دقائق الطريقين، وأنى لهم تصور نزول الروحانيات وتشخصها بالجسمانيات، كما أخبر التنزيل عنه: فَأَرْسَلْنَا إِلَيْهَا رُوحَنَا فَتَمَثَّلَ لَهَا بَشَرًا سَوِيًّا [مريم: 17]. وكيف يستقيم على مذهب المتكلم تمثل الروح بالشخص البشري: أبانعدم الروح ويوجد الشخص، وليس ذلك من التمثل في شيء، أو بأن يستعمل الروح شخصاً موجوداً بشرياً ولم يكن ذلك تمثلاً أيضاً، بل تناسخاً. وإذا لم يمكنه تقرير التمثل والتشكل؛ أعني تمثل الروحاني بالجسماني، كيف يمكنه تصور الأمر الأزلي يتمثل باللسان العربي تارة واللسان السرياني طوراً حتى يجب أن يقال: كلام الله كما يجب أن يقال: «هذا جبريل جاءكم ليعلمكم دينكم، ثم لباس جبريل يتبدل ولا تتبدل حقيقته التي هو بها جبريل، ولباس الكلام الأزلي يتبدل ولا تتبدل حقيقته التي هو بها كلام وأمر ونهي واحد أزلي. ﴿ وَإِنْ أَحَدٌ مِّنَ المُشْرِكِينَ اسْتَجَارَكَ فَأَجِرْهُ حَتَّى يَسْمَعَ كَلَامَ اللهِ ﴾ حق في حق المشرك المستجير، و ﴿ يَا مُوسَىٰ إِنِّي اصْطَفَيْتُكَ عَلَى النَّاسِ بِرِسَالَاتِي وَبِكَلَامِي ﴾ صدق في حق الكليم المستنير. فبين الكلامين فرق ما بين القدم والفرق وبين السمعين صرف ما بين الولاية والصرف. ولو كان الكلام في الموضعين حروفاً منظومة أصواتاً مقطعة بطل الاصطفاء على الناس وحصل التساوي واستماع الناس والخناس، ولكن حكم الكليم في خطابه يا موسى حكم الطريد في طرده يا فرعون، ولكان الذي لا يسمع من موسى أسعد حالاً من موسى؛ إذ سمع من الشجرة التي هي جماد، وفي الموضعين الكلام حروف وأصوات.
The End of the Stride in the Science of Kalam. About time: whatever will exist has already existed, and the relation of address to Him at that time is like His relation to Him at this time. Whoever can remove time from the core of his heart finds it easy to perceive intellectual meanings, and easy to know the connection of eternal knowledge to known things, and the eternal command to commanded things, and knows that difference refers back to expressions and phrasings. Look how we point to flashes of truths in the language of the two groups, though they are removed from the subtleties of the two paths. How can they conceive the descent of spiritual beings and their embodiment in physical forms, as the Revelation informed about it: 'Then We sent to her Our Spirit, and it appeared to her as a well-proportioned man' [Maryam: 17]. How can the theologian's doctrine accommodate the spirit's manifestation in a human person: either the spirit is annihilated and the person exists—that is not manifestation at all—or the spirit uses an already existing human person and that is not manifestation either, but rather transmigration. If he cannot establish manifestation and embodiment—meaning the spiritual manifesting in physical—how can he conceive the eternal command manifesting in the Arabic tongue one time and the Syriac tongue another time, so that it must be said: 'the speech of God' just as it must be said: 'This is Jibril coming to teach you your religion; then Jibril's garment changes but his essence, by which he is Jibril, does not change; and the garment of eternal speech changes but its essence, by which it is speech and command and prohibition, one eternal, does not change.' 'And if any one of the polytheists seeks your protection, then grant him protection so that he may hear the speech of Allah' is true regarding the polytheist who seeks protection, and 'O Moses, indeed I have chosen you over the people with My messages and My speech' is true regarding the intimate interlocutor enlightened. So between the two speeches is the difference between pre-eternity and createdness, and between the two hearings is the distinction between sainthood and degradation. And if speech in both places was organized letters and segmented sounds, the election over the people would be invalidated, equality would occur, and the hearing of the people and the devil would be the same. But the state of the intimate interlocutor in his address 'O Moses' is like the state of the outcast in his expulsion 'O Pharaoh'; and the one who does not hear from Moses would be in a better state than Moses, since he heard from the tree, which is inanimate. And in both places speech is letters and sounds.
Source pages p. 291

Commentary
This passage is a complex theological argument about the nature of God's eternal speech (الكلام الأزلي) and its manifestation in created sounds and letters. The author begins by establishing a principle: the relation of God's address to any time is uniform—past, present, future—because God's knowledge and command are eternal, not subject to change. Whoever grasps this can understand how eternal attributes relate to temporal things without alteration. He then introduces the problem of how spiritual realities (الروحانيات) can manifest in physical forms (الجسمانيات), using the Quranic example of the angel Gabriel appearing to Mary as a human (Q19:17). He challenges the theologian (المتكلم) to explain this: if the spirit (the angel) is annihilated and a person is created anew, that is not manifestation but substitution; if the spirit uses an existing human body, that is not manifestation but transmigration (تناسخ). Since the theologian cannot properly account for angelic manifestation, how can he conceive of the eternal command (الأمر الأزلي) manifesting in different human languages (Arabic, Syriac) without change? The author then introduces an analogy: just as the angel Jibril appears in different forms (clothing) while his essence remains unchanged, so the eternal speech 'wears' different garment of sounds and words while remaining one eternal essence. He cites two Quranic verses: one about a polytheist hearing 'the speech of Allah' (Q9:6) and one about Moses being addressed directly by God (Q7:144). Both instances involve sounds and letters, yet the author insists there is a fundamental difference: the first is a created temporal speech, while the second is a manifestation of the eternal speech. If both were merely created sounds, there would be no special distinction for Moses. In fact, the polytheist would be equal to Moses, and even an inanimate tree would be superior to Moses because it 'spoke' to him. The author then makes a striking concession: 'And in both places speech is letters and sounds' (وفي الموضعين الكلام حروف وأصوات). This seems to acknowledge that the phenomenal form is the same, but the ontological reality differs—one is created, one is eternal. The passage emphasizes the Ash'ari doctrine that God's speech is one eternal attribute, which expresses itself in temporal sounds without division or change.

Misconception: One might think that if eternal speech appears in sounds, it becomes created and multiple.
Correction: The author clarifies that the manifestation is like a garment: the outward form changes, but the essence of eternal speech remains one and unchanging, just as Jibril's essence does not change when he appears in different forms.

Key Takeaway
Eternal speech manifests in temporal sounds without change, just as Gabriel's outward appearance changes while his essence remains one.

The Eternal Speech of God: Beyond Letters and Sounds
The author argues that God's eternal speech is not composed of letters and sounds.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author argues that God's eternal speech is not composed of letters and sounds, and how this preserves the distinction between the eternal and temporal.

Core Idea
God's speech is an eternal attribute, not temporal letters and sounds.

The author tackles a central theological puzzle: how can God's speech be one and eternal, yet appear in the Quran as human words? He argues that God's speech itself is not composed of letters and sounds, which are temporal, but is an eternal attribute. The passage uses two Quranic verses to show that the special status of Moses (being chosen to hear God's speech) would be meaningless if God's speech were like temporal speech.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice how the author uses Quranic verses as evidence.
2
Identify the two Quranic examples
The author cites two verses: one about a polytheist hearing the word of God (Surah 9:6) and one about Moses being chosen and addressed (Surah 7:144). Note how each is used.
The first example is about hearing; the second about being chosen.
3
Understand the logical argument
The author argues: if God's speech in both cases were composed of letters and sounds, then Moses' selection would be invalid and everyone would have equal hearing. This shows that God's eternal speech is not such.
The key phrase is 'بطل الاصطفاء' (the selection would be invalid).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يتبدل ولا تتبدل حقيقته التي هو بها كلام وأمر ونهي واحد أزلي، وَإِنْ أَحَدٌ مِّنَ المُشْرِكِينَ اسْتَجَارَكَ فَأَجِرْهُ حَتَّى يَسْمَعَ كَلَمَ اللهِ حق في حق المشرك المستجير، ويا موسى إني اصطفيتك على الناس برسالاتي وبكلامي، صدق في حق الكليم المستنير، فبين الكلامين فرق ما بين القدم والفرق وبين السمعين صرف ما بين الولاية والصرف ولو كان الكلام في الموضعين حروفا منظومة أصواتا مقطعة بطل الاصطفاء على الناس وحصل التساوي واستماع الناس والخناس، ولكن حكم الكليم في خطابه يا موسى حكم الطريد في طرده يا فرعون، ولكان الذي لا يسمع من موسى أسعد حالا من موسى؛ إذ سمع من الشجرة التي هي جماد، وفي الموضعين الكلام حروف وأصوات.
He does not change, nor does His essence change, by which He is speech, command, and prohibition, one eternal. And 'if any of the polytheists seeks your protection, then grant him protection so that he may hear the word of Allah' is true regarding the polytheist seeking protection. And 'O Moses, I have chosen you over the people by My messages and My speech' is true regarding the illuminated interlocutor. Between the two speeches is the difference between the eternal and the temporal, and between the two hearings is the distinction between the closeness and the withdrawal. And the one who does not hear from Moses would be better off than Moses, since he heard from a tree which is inanimate. And in both places, speech is letters and sounds.

Commentary
This passage defines the author's position on the nature of God's speech. It is an argument that God's speech is an eternal attribute, not composed of letters and sounds. The author uses two Quranic verses: the polytheist hearing the Quran (Surah 9:6) and Moses hearing God's speech (Surah 7:144). He argues that if speech in both instances were composed of letters and sounds, then the special status of Moses (being chosen to hear God's speech) would be nullified, and everyone—including the polytheist and even Satan (al-khannas)—would have equal hearing. The distinction between the two hearings is thus essential. The author concludes by stating that in both places, the speech appears as letters and sounds (i.e., in the temporal realm), but the eternal speech itself is beyond that.

Four Mu'tazila Objections to Eternal Speech
The Mu'tazila present four arguments against the concept of eternal speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four logical objections raised by the Mu'tazila against the Ash'ari doctrine of God's eternal speech.

Core Idea
Eternal speech is impossible because it requires an addressee and distinct speech types.

The Mu'tazila, in their rational critique, present four arguments that challenge the coherence of an eternal speech attributed to God. They contend that command, prohibition, information, and inquiry cannot exist without a real recipient, and that speech types are mutually exclusive. This lesson examines these objections in detail.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Note the structure: the Mu'tazila present a dilemma and then four objections.
The passage is dense; reading slowly helps.
2
Identify each objection
Find the four objections. They are introduced with 'One: ...', 'The second: ...', 'The third: ...', 'The fourth: ...'. Note the key point of each.
3
Understand the logic behind each
For each objection, ask: Why does the Mu'tazila think this is a problem for the Ash'ari? What assumption are they attacking?
For example, objection 1 attacks the need for an addressee.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The Mu'tazila conclude that the Ash'ari position collapses the distinctions between speech types. Consider whether their reasoning is sound from an Ash'ari perspective.
This requires knowledge of the Ash'ari response, which is not in this passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالت المعتزلة : إذا أثبتم كلامًا أزليا فإما أن تحكموا بأن كلام الله أمر ونهي وخبر واستخبار في الأزل، وإما أن لا تحكموا به، فإن حكمتم به فقد أحلتم من وجوه أحدها: أن من حكم الأمر أن يصادف مأمورًا ولم يكن في الأزل مخاطب لأن يحث على أمر ويزجر عن آخر، ويستحيل كون المعدوم مأمورًا. والوجه الثاني : أن الكلام مع نفسه من غير مخاطب سفه في الشاهد، والنداء لشخص لا وجود له من أمحل ما ينسب إلى الحكيم. والثالث : أن الخطاب مع موسى عليه السلام غير الخطاب مع النبي عليه السلام، ومناهج الكلامين مع الرسولين مختلفة، ويستحيل أن يكون معنى واحد هو في نفسه كلام مع شخص على معان ومناهج، وكلام مع شخص آخر على معان ومناهج أخر، ثم يكون الكلامان شيئًا واحدًا ومعنى واحدًا. والرابع : أن الخبرين عن أحوال الأمتين مختلف لاختلاف حال الأمتين، وكيف يتصور أن تكون حالتان مختلفتان فيخبر عنهما بخبر واحد؟ وكيف يكون الخبر أمرًا ونهيا؟ وكيف يكون أمر ونهي خبرًا واستخبارًا ووعدا ووعيدا؟ وأنكم إن حكمتم بأن الكلام واحد فقد رفعتم أقسام الكلام، ولا يعقل كلام إلا وأن يكون إما أمرًا ونهيًا أو إما خبرًا واستخبارًا، وردكم أقسام الكلام إلى أوصاف واعتبارات تارة، وإلى تعبيرات وعبارات أخرى غير سديد.
The Mu'tazila said: If you affirm eternal speech, then either you rule that God's speech is command, prohibition, information, and inquiry in eternity, or you do not rule that. If you rule that, then you have become impossible in several respects: One: that command requires that it encounter a commanded one, and there was no addressee in eternity, because it urges to something and forbids from another, and it is impossible for the nonexistent to be commanded. The second: that speaking to oneself without an addressee is foolishness in the empirical world, and calling to a person who has no existence is among the most absurd things attributed to the wise. The third: that the address to Moses, peace be upon him, is not the address to the Prophet, peace be upon him, and the methods of the two speeches to the two messengers are different, and it is impossible that one meaning is itself speech to one person according to meanings and methods, and speech to another person according to other meanings and methods, then the two speeches be one thing and one meaning. The fourth: that the two reports about the conditions of the two communities are different due to the difference of the conditions of the two communities. How can it be conceived that two different conditions be reported by one report? And how can information be command and prohibition? And how can command and prohibition be information and inquiry, promise and threat? And if you rule that speech is one, then you have abolished the divisions of speech, and speech is not conceivable unless it is either command and prohibition or information and inquiry. And your reduction of the divisions of speech to descriptions and considerations on one hand, and to expressions and phrases on the other, is not sound.
Source pages p. 292

Commentary
This passage presents four objections from the Mu'tazila against the Ash'ari doctrine of eternal speech. The objections are enumerated in the source. First objection: If God's speech is eternal and includes commands, then there must be a commanded one in eternity, but there was none; commanding the nonexistent is impossible. Second objection: Speaking to oneself without an addressee is foolish in human experience; attributing such to God is absurd. Third objection: God's address to Moses differs from that to Muhammad (peace be upon them); it is impossible for one eternal meaning to be simultaneously two different addresses. Fourth objection: Reports about different communities must differ; one report cannot convey different conditions. Moreover, speech types (command, prohibition, information, inquiry) are distinct and cannot be reduced to mere considerations and expressions as the Ash'aris claim. The Mu'tazila conclude that the Ash'ari position is untenable.

Key Terms
الأمر والنهي
الخبر والاستخبار
المخاطب
أقسام الكلام
الاعتبارات
المعتزلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT one of the Mu'tazila objections presented in this passage?
A) Command requires an addressee, which did not exist in eternity.
B) Speaking to oneself is foolishness.
C) One speech cannot be both command and information.
D) The eternal speech must be in a particular language.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila argue that the Ash'ari doctrine of eternal speech leads to logical contradictions regarding the necessity of addressees, the diversity of speech types, and the impossibility of one meaning serving multiple contexts.

How does the Ash'ari claim that God's speech is one eternal meaning challenge your own assumptions about how God communicates? Will you accept that God's speech transcends your understanding, or will you insist on confining it to your own logic?
?
The Mu'tazila argue that it is 'foolishness' to speak to oneself or to call to a nonexistent person. Do you sometimes judge God by human standards, expecting His actions to conform to what seems reasonable to you? How can you guard against this tendency in your own theology? What step will you take today to submit your intellect to divine revelation?

Two Rational Arguments Against the Unity of Divine Speech
Two arguments: one based on interlocutor absurdity, one on content diversity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how two rational arguments—based on the absurdity of speaking to oneself and on the diversity of revealed content—refute the claim that God's speech is a single undifferentiated entity.

Core Idea
If God's speech were one, it would entail absurdities like speaking to oneself or identical content to different prophets.

After establishing the concept of divine speech, the author presents two arguments that challenge the idea that God's speech is a single entity. These arguments draw on observable human experience and the clear differences in the content of revelations. By examining these objections, we see why theologians insisted on the multiplicity of God's speech.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the literal translation. Pay attention to the structure: the author enumerates two 'aspects' (وجه), each beginning with 'والثاني' and 'والثالث'.
Notice how each argument is introduced distinctly.
2
Identify the first argument (second aspect)
The first argument states that speaking to oneself without an interlocutor is foolish (سفه) in human experience, and calling a nonexistent person is absurd for a wise being. This would apply if God's speech were a single undifferentiated entity addressing no one in particular.
The argument relies on analogy with human speech (شاهد).
3
Identify the second argument (third aspect)
The second argument points to the difference between the speech addressed to Moses and that addressed to Muhammad. Since the content and methods of address differ, it is impossible for a single meaning to be simultaneously speech to two persons with different contents.
This argument emphasizes the diversity in revelation.
4
Reflect on the cumulative force
Consider why these two arguments together undermine the doctrine of numerical unity of divine speech. They show that such unity leads to logical contradictions or absurd consequences.
The arguments are not exhaustive but sufficient for the author's refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والوجه الثاني : أن الكلام مع نفسه من غير مخاطب سفه في الشاهد، والنداء لشخص لا وجود له من أمحل ما ينسب إلى الحكيم. والثالث : أن الخطاب مع موسى عليه السلام غير الخطاب مع النبي عليه السلام، ومناهج الكلامين مع الرسولين مختلفة، ويستحيل أن يكون معنى واحد هو في نفسه كلام مع شخص على معان ومناهج، وكلام مع شخص آخر على معان ومناهج أخر، ثم يكون الكلامان شيئًا واحدًا ومعنى واحدًا.
And the second aspect: that speech with oneself without an interlocutor is foolishness in the witnessed realm, and calling out to a person who has no existence is among the most baseless things attributed to the wise. And the third: that the address with Moses, peace be upon him, is not the address with the Prophet, peace be upon him, and the methods of the two speeches with the two messengers are different, and it is impossible for one meaning to be in itself speech with a person upon meanings and methods, and speech with another person upon other meanings and methods, and then the two speeches be one thing and one meaning.
Source pages p. 293

Commentary
This passage presents two distinct arguments (the second and third 'aspects') against the claim that God's speech is a single undifferentiated entity. The first argument (the second aspect) points out that if God's speech is one, then when He addresses no creature, it would be like speaking to oneself without an interlocutor, which is foolish (سفه) in human experience. Moreover, calling to a nonexistent person is among the most absurd things one could attribute to a wise being. The second argument (the third aspect) focuses on the difference between the speech addressed to Moses and that addressed to Muhammad. Since the contents and contexts are different, it is impossible for a single meaning to be simultaneously speech to two different persons with different meanings and methods; that would require the same thing to be two different things.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think these arguments deny God's ability to communicate multiple messages; they actually deny that all speech is numerically one.
Correction: The arguments do not reject that God speaks to prophets; they reject the idea that all of God's speech is a single undifferentiated entity. The diversity of revelations is preserved.

The diversity in God's addresses to different prophets proves that His speech is not a single undifferentiated entity.

Fourth Argument: Impossibility of Unified Reports
An argument demonstrating the impossibility of unified speech through the diversity of speech categories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why different types of speech (commands, reports, etc.) cannot be reduced to a single divine speech, and the logical consequences of such a claim.

Core Idea
If all speech is one, the distinct categories of speech vanish.

This lesson presents a powerful argument from our author against the view that all of God's speech is a single, undifferentiated entity. By examining the nature of reports, commands, prohibitions, and other speech acts, the argument shows that these are inherently distinct realities. If we abolish these distinctions, we lose the very meaning of speech itself. Read on to see how the author uses logical reasoning to defend the multiplicity of divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the series of rhetorical questions that drive the argument.
2
Identify the key distinction
The author argues that reports about different conditions must differ. Why? Because the content differs. How could a single report cover two different states?
3
Understand the conclusion
The author states that if speech is one, the categories of speech are abolished. Reflect on why this is a logical consequence: if all speech is one, then command, prohibition, report, etc., would be identical, which is absurd.
The categories are: command, prohibition, report, questioning, promise, threat.
4
Engage with the critique
The author criticizes the opponent's move of reducing these categories to mere 'considerations' or 'expressions'. Why is this unsatisfactory? Because the categories are distinct realities (ذوات مختلفة), not just mental distinctions.
This is a key metaphysical point: speech types are real, not just conceptual.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والرابع : أن الخبرين عن أحوال الأمتين مختلف لاختلاف حال الأمتين، وكيف يتصور أن تكون حالتان مختلفتان فيخبر عنهما بخبر واحد؟ وكيف يكون الخبر أمرًا ونهيًا؟ وكيف يكون أمر ونهي خبرًا واستخبارًا ووعدا ووعيدا؟ وأنكم إن حكمتم بأن الكلام واحد فقد رفعتم أقسام الكلام، ولا يعقل كلام إلا وأن يكون إما أمرًا ونهيًا أو إما خبرًا واستخبارًا، وردكم أقسام الكلام إلى أوصاف واعتبارات تارة، وإلى تعبيرات وعبارات أخرى غير سديد، أما الاعتبارات العقلية فباطلة؛ لأن المعقول من أقسام الكلام ذوات مختلفة وحقائق متباينة.
And the fourth: that the two reports about the conditions of the two communities are different because of the difference of the condition of the two communities, and how can it be conceived that two different conditions be reported with one report? And how can a report be a command and a prohibition? And how can command and prohibition be a report and a questioning and a promise and a threat? And if you judge that speech is one, you have abolished the categories of speech, and speech cannot be conceived except that it is either command and prohibition or report and questioning, and your reduction of the categories of speech to descriptions and considerations sometimes, and to expressions and utterances at other times, is not sound; as for the intellectual considerations, they are false because what is understood of the categories of speech are different essences and distinct realities.
Source pages p. 293

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth argument against the unity of divine speech. It begins by noting that reports about two different communities must be different because the conditions differ. It then poses rhetorical questions: how can a single report cover two different states? How can a report be simultaneously a command and a prohibition? How can a command be also a report, a request, a promise, and a threat? The author argues that if one insists that speech is one, one abolishes the very categories of speech (أقسام الكلام), because speech is only conceivable as either command/prohibition or report/questioning. Then the author criticizes the opponent's move of reducing these categories to mere 'descriptions and considerations' or 'expressions and utterances'—this is not sound. The 'intellectual considerations' (الاعتبارات العقلية) are false because the various types of speech are distinct essences (ذوات مختلفة) and realities (حقائق متباينة).

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This argument is merely rhetorical and lacks logical substance.
Correction: The argument is a logical demonstration: it shows that unity of speech leads to the abolition of necessary distinctions between types of speech, which is a logical impossibility. The rhetorical questions are used to highlight inherent contradictions.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The diversity of speech categories (command, report, etc.) demonstrates that divine speech cannot be a single undifferentiated entity; otherwise, these categories would lose their distinct meanings.

The Impossibility of Unified Divine Speech: Stories and Commands
Illustrates impossibility of unified speech using prophetic narratives and type distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why different prophetic narratives and distinct commands cannot be considered a single unified speech, and why the nature of reports and commands are fundamentally different.

Core Idea
Different stories and commands cannot be the same speech; their very natures differ.

The opponent argues that all divine speech is a single unified entity. But this leads to absurdity: the story of Joseph cannot be the same as the story of Moses. Moreover, a report (khabar) and a command (amr) have opposite functions—one informs, the other demands. How can they be one? Let's see the author's argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the rhetorical questions challenging the opponent.
The passage uses concrete examples from the Quran.
2
Identify the two arguments
The author first gives examples of different stories and commands. Then he contrasts the definitions of khabar and amr. Identify each part.
3
Reflect on the core distinction
Consider why the author insists that khabar and amr cannot be unified. What is the essential difference between informing and demanding?
This distinction is not merely linguistic but real.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن القصة التي جرت ليوسف وإخوته - صلوات الله عليهم أجمعين غير القصة التي جرت لآدم ونوح وإبراهيم وموسى وعيسى فالخبر عما جرى في حق شخص كيف يكون عين الخبر الذي جرى في حق شخص آخر؟ والأوامر والنواهي التي توجهت على قوم أخر في دور نبي مخصوص غير الأوامر التي توجهت على قوم أخر في دور آخر، فكيف يمكنكم القول باتحاد الأخبار كلها على اختلافها في خبر واحد، والقول باتحاد الأوامر على تفاوتها في أمر واحد؟ ثم كيف يمكنكم الجمع بين معنى الخبر وحقيقته أنه خبر عما كان أو سيكون من غير اقتضاء وطلب وبين معنى الأمر وحقيقته أنه اقتضاء وطلب لأمر لم يكن حتى يكون، فليس في الخبر حكم واقتضاء وليس في الأمر خبر وإنباء وبين النوعين فرق ظاهر؟ فكيف يمكن القول باتحادهما؟
For the story that occurred with Joseph and his brothers – prayers of God be upon them all – is not the story that occurred with Adam, Noah, Abraham, Moses, and Jesus; so how can the report about what happened concerning one person be identical to the report about what happened concerning another person? And the commands and prohibitions that were directed to one people in the era of a specific prophet are not the commands that were directed to another people in another era; so how can you claim the unification of all reports despite their differences into one report, and the claim of the unification of commands despite their diversity into one command? Then how can you combine the meaning and reality of the report, that it is news about what was or will be without implication and demand, with the meaning and reality of the command, that it is an implication and demand for something that was not yet to be? For there is no judgment and implication in the report, and there is no news and information in the command, and between the two types there is a clear difference. So how can you claim their unification?
Source pages p. 294

Commentary
This passage continues the refutation of the unity of divine speech by concrete examples and the fundamental distinction between khabar (report) and amr (command). The author argues that the story of Joseph is different from that of Adam, Noah, etc., so the report about one cannot be identical to the report about another. Similarly, commands given to different communities through different prophets are distinct. It is therefore impossible that all reports are one report or all commands one command. Furthermore, the very nature of a report is to inform about what was or will be, without implying or demanding anything, while a command is precisely an implication and demand for something to occur. These two types have a clear difference; hence they cannot be unified. The rhetorical question 'how can you claim their unification?' drives home the point.

خبر (report)
أمر (command)
نهي (prohibition)
اقتضاء (implication/demand)
طلب (demand)
حكم (judgment)
إنباء (information)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that the distinction between khabar and amr is merely linguistic or a matter of form, not essence.
Correction: The author insists that the difference is real and essential: khabar informs without demanding, while amr demands without primarily informing. They are inherently different types of speech.

Checkpoint

B) A report informs without demanding, while a command demands.
C) A report is about the past, a command about the future.
D) There is no real difference.

Key Takeaway
The diversity of divine speech in narratives and commandments proves it cannot be a single undifferentiated entity; reports and commands have intrinsically different meanings.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Are you missing the full purpose of revelation by ignoring one side? Commit to finding one command you have been treating as mere news and respond to it as a personal demand.

Unity in Genus and Difference in Species of Speech
Affirms unity in genus and species difference; refutes reduction to report or command.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between genus and species in divine speech and why reducing report to command or vice versa is invalid.

Core Idea
Speech types share a genus but not a species; reducing one to another abolishes essential meanings.

In this passage, the author resolves a theological debate about the nature of God's speech. He affirms that while all speech belongs to the same genus (الكلامية), different types like report and command are distinct species. He then warns against two reductionist errors: reducing all speech to report, which eliminates the imperative aspect, or reducing it to command, which destroys the narrative aspect. This lesson unpacks this crucial distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the main claim
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. The author makes a positive statement about unity in genus but difference in species using analogies.
Notice the analogies: animality/humanity and accidentality/color-ness.
2
Understand the analogy of animality and humanity
Just as humanity and animality share the genus 'animality' but differ in species, so report and command share the genus 'speechness' but differ in species.
3
Examine the two reductionist views
The author criticizes those who reduce all speech to report, and those who reduce it to command. He explains the negative consequences of each reduction.
The first abolishes implication and nullifies judgment and demand; the second abolishes the meaning of report and nullifies stories.
4
Reflect on why both types are necessary
The author concludes that both types exist in the genus of speech and are mentioned in divine books. This shows that a balanced view is required.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نعم، هما يتحدان في حقيقة الكلامية لكنهما يختلفان بالنوعية كالحيوانية والإنسانية والعرضية واللونية، فمن رد الكلام بأنواعه وأقسامه إلى الخبر فقد أبطل الاقتضاء وعطل الحكم والطلب ومن رد الكلام إلى الأمر فقد أبطل معنى الخبر وعطل القصص، ومن المعلوم أن النوعين موجودان في جنس الكلام، ومذكوران في الكتب الإلهية، وأما من رد الاختلاف والكثرة فيهما إلى العبارات فقد أبعد النجعة، فإن العبارات إن طابقت المعاني خبرا للمخبر، وأمرا لمأمور، ونهيا...
Yes, they are united in the reality of speechness (الكلامية) but they differ in specificity (النوعية) like animality and humanity and accidentally and color-ness. So whoever reduces speech with its types and categories to report has abolished implication and nullified judgment and demand, and whoever reduces speech to command has abolished the meaning of report and nullified the stories. And it is known that the two types exist in the genus of speech and are mentioned in the divine books. And as for whoever reduces the difference and multiplicity in them to expressions, he has gone far astray, for expressions, if they correspond to meanings, are a report for the one informed, and a command for the one commanded, and a prohibition...
Source pages p. 294

Commentary
This passage provides a nuanced conclusion. The author concedes that different types of speech (like report and command) are united under the common genus of 'speechness' (الكلامية), similar to how humanity and animality are both under animality. However, they differ in species (النوعية), just as different colors differ. The author then criticizes two reductionist extremes: reducing all speech to report abolishes the essential aspect of demand; reducing all speech to command abolishes the informative aspect and the stories. Both types are present in the genus of speech and are attested in divine scriptures. Finally, the author refutes the view that the differences are merely a matter of different expressions (عبارات) for the same meaning. Such a view is misguided because expressions, when they match meanings, actually correspond to the type of speech they convey—report for informing, command for commanding, prohibition for forbidding, etc. The text cuts off here, but the argument is clear: the diversity of speech types is real and not reducible to mere verbal variation.

Key Terms
حقيقة الكلامية
النوعية
جنس الكلام
الخبر
الأمر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All divine speech is essentially one type (e.g., only report or only command).
Correction: The author argues that speech has a common genus but distinct species; reducing one to the other undermines essential aspects.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the consequence of reducing all speech to report?
A) It strengthens the meaning of demand.
B) It abolishes implication and nullifies judgment and demand.
C) It preserves both report and command.
D) It has no effect.

Key Takeaway
Speech types share a genus but are distinct species; reducing one to another destroys essential meanings.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The analogy in this lesson uses animal/human and color/whiteness to explain genus and species. When you consider Allah's attributes, do you tend to reduce them to a single dimension? How does this lesson push you to embrace the richness of each attribute without canceling others? Ask yourself: where have I unconsciously nullified an essential aspect of Allah's revelation by oversimplifying it?

Refutation of Reducing Speech Differences to Expressions
Refutes the view that differences in speech are merely expressions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the view that differences in speech types are merely verbal is misguided, and how expressions actually correspond to the meanings they convey.

Core Idea
Differences in speech are not mere expressions; expressions correspond to the intended meaning.

After establishing that speech types are distinct species, the author now addresses a more subtle error: the claim that the difference between report and command is only in the expressions, not in the meanings. He shows this is misguided because expressions, when they match meanings, are tailored to the type of speech—report for informing, command for commanding. This lesson examines this refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation
Read the Arabic and translation. The author starts with 'وأما من رد' and strongly condemns this view.
The phrase 'فقد أبعد النجعة' means he has gone far astray.
2
Understand the reason
The author explains that if expressions match meanings, then the expression itself indicates the type: a report expression conveys a report meaning, a command expression conveys a command meaning.
The text cuts off after 'ونهيا', but the argument is clear.
3
Reflect on the implications
This refutation shows that the diversity of speech types is real and rooted in meanings, not just words.
This guards against a purely linguistic reduction of theological attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Commentary
This passage provides a nuanced conclusion. The author concedes that different types of speech (like report and command) are united under the common genus of 'speechness' (الكلامية), similar to how humanity and animality are both under animality. However, they differ in species (النوعية), just as different colors differ. If the diversity of speech is real in God's attribute, what does that say about His care to communicate different types of guidance to us? Are you responding appropriately to each type—reports with belief, commands with obedience, prohibitions with avoidance?
?
The author says reducing differences to expressions is 'far astray'. Is there any area of your faith where you treat the outward form as all that matters, neglecting the inner meaning? Commit to one practical step this week to align your understanding more deeply with what God intends.

The Distinct Realities of Divine Reports and Commands
Refutation of reducing all speech to a single type via examples and definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that divine reports and commands are distinct realities based on their subjects and essential definitions, and why they cannot be reduced to a single type.

Core Idea
Reports and commands are fundamentally different — they differ in meaning and cannot be one.

The author begins by illustrating the obvious diversity: the story of Yusuf is not the same as the story of Adam, and the commands to one community differ from those to another. He then defines a report as informing without entailment, and a command as entailing a request. This lesson establishes the foundational argument against reductionism in divine speech.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the examples of different reports and commands.
The author uses rhetorical questions to expose contradictions.
2
Identify the examples
The story of Yusuf vs. Adam, and commands/prohibitions for different prophets/communities are given as evidence of diversity.
3
Understand the definitions
A report informs about what was or will be without entailment; a command entails a request for something not yet existing. Note the key terms: khabar, amr, iqtida', talab.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The author asks: how can one claim unification when definitions and contents differ? Conclude that reports and commands are distinct species.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن المعقول من أقسام الكلام ذوات مختلفة وحقائق متباينة، فإن القصة التي جرت ليوسف وإخوته صلوات الله عليهم غير القصة التي جرت لآدم ونوح وإبراهيم وموسى وعيسى فالخبر عما جرى في حق شخص كيف يكون عين الخبر الذي جرى في حق شخص آخر؟ والأوامر والنواهي التي توجهت على قوم أخر في دور نبي مخصوص غير الأوامر التي توجهت على قوم أخر في دور آخر، فكيف يمكنكم القول باتحاد الأخبار كلها على اختلافها في خبر واحد، والقول باتحاد الأوامر على تفاوتها في أمر واحد؟ ثم كيف يمكنكم الجمع بين معنى الخبر وحقيقته أنه خبر عما كان أو سيكون من غير اقتضاء وطلب وبين معنى الأمر وحقيقته أنه اقتضاء وطلب لأمر لم يكن حتى يكون، فليس في الخبر حكم واقتضاء وليس في الأمر خبر وإنباء وبين النوعين فرق ظاهر؟ فكيف يمكن القول باتحادهما؟

نعم، هما يتحدان في حقيقة الكلامية لكنهما يختلفان بالنوعية كالحيوانية والإنسانية والعرضية واللونية، فمن رد الكلام بأنواعه وأقسامه إلى الخبر فقد أبطل الاقتضاء وعطل الحكم والطلب ومن رد الكلام إلى الأمر فقد أبطل معنى الخبر وعطل القصص، ومن المعلوم أن النوعين موجودان في جنس الكلام، ومذكوران في الكتب الإلهية، وأما من رد الاختلاف والكثرة فيهما إلى العبارات فقد أبعد النجعة، فإن العبارات إن طابقت المعاني خبرا لمخبر، وأمرا لمأمور، ونهيا لمنهي، فقد تعددت المعاني تعدد العبارات، وإن لم تطابق فليست هي تعبيرات عنها، وإنما هي عبارات لا معنى لها وذلك كلام المجانين.

وأما استرواحكم إلى تمثل الروحاني بالجسماني وتشكل الملك بالبشر، وظهور المعنى بالعبارات فذلك في أسماعنا شبه كلمات وكلمات فارغة، فما التمثل والتشكل؟ وكيف الظهور والتبين؟ حققوا لنا ذلك إن كانت العبارة مشتملة على حقيقة، وإلا فالمعلومات لا تحتمل أمثال هذه المجازفات. والذي عندنا أن جبريل شخص لطيف يتكاثف فيتراءى للبصر كالهواء اللطيف الذي لا يرى، فيتكاثف فيرى سحابا، أو نقول بإعدام وإيجاد لا يتمثل ولا يتشخص، وبالجملة جوهر واحد لا يصير جواهر إلا بانضمام جواهر إليه ونحن لا نعقل من الجواهر إلا المتحيز، والمتحيزان لا يتداخلان، فلا معنى لما تمسكتم به.
For what is understood from the divisions of speech are different essences and distinct realities. The story that occurred with Yusuf and his brothers—may God's blessings be upon them all—is not the story that occurred with Adam, Noah, Abraham, Moses, and Jesus. So how can the report about what occurred concerning one person be the very report that occurred concerning another person? And the commands and prohibitions that were directed at one people in the era of a particular prophet are not the commands that were directed at another people in another era. So how can you claim the unification of all reports, despite their differences, into a single report, and the unification of commands, despite their disparities, into a single command? Then how can you combine the meaning and reality of a report—that it is a report about what was or will be, without entailment and request—with the meaning and reality of a command—that it is an entailment and request for something that was not until it becomes? For there is no judgment and entailment in a report, and there is no report or notification in a command, and between the two types there is an apparent difference. So how can one claim their unification? Yes, they are united in the reality of speech-ness, but they differ in kind, like animality and humanity and accidental-ness and color-ness. Whoever reduces speech, with its types and divisions, to a report has nullified entailment and canceled judgment and request; and whoever reduces speech to a command has nullified the meaning of a report and canceled stories. And it is known that both types exist in the genus of speech and are mentioned in the divine books. As for whoever reduces the difference and multiplicity in them to expressions, he has gone far astray. For if the expressions correspond to the meanings—a report about that which is reported, a command to that which is commanded, and a prohibition to that which is prohibited—then the meanings are as multiple as the expressions; and if they do not correspond, then they are not expressions of them; rather, they are expressions without meaning, and that is the speech of madmen. As for your resorting to the likening of the spiritual to the physical, and the angel taking the form of a human, and the manifestation of meaning through expressions—that, to our ears, is like words and empty words. So what is this likening and taking form? And how is the manifestation and clarification? Substantiate that for us if the expression contains a reality; otherwise, known things cannot bear such reckless assertions. What we hold is that Gabriel is a subtle person who becomes dense and appears to the eye like the subtle air that is not seen; then he becomes dense and is seen as a cloud. Or we say by annihilation and creation, without taking form or becoming individuated. In sum, a single substance does not become multiple substances except by the addition of other substances to it, and we only understand from substances that which is extended in space (mutehayyiz), and two extended things cannot interpenetrate. So there is no meaning to what you have clung to.
Source pages p. 294

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the view that all of God's speech (kalam) is of a single type—either all news (akhbar) or all commands (awamir). The author begins by arguing that the reports (akhbar) about different prophets and events are distinct realities; the story of Yusuf is not the same as the story of Adam, so how could the report about one be identical to the report about another? Similarly, commands and prohibitions directed to different communities in different prophetic eras are not identical. The author then asks how one can claim the unification of all reports or commands into one. He further highlights the definitional difference: a report informs about what was or will be without entailment or request, whereas a command entails a request for something that does not yet exist. Thus, reports and commands are fundamentally different and cannot be united. The author then concedes that they are united in the genus of speech (kalamiyya) but differ in species, like animality and humanity. Those who reduce all speech to report nullify entailment and command; those who reduce it to command nullify the meaning of report and cancel stories. Both types exist in the genus and are found in scripture. He then criticizes those who attribute the diversity merely to different expressions, arguing that if expressions correspond to meanings, the meanings are as diverse as the expressions; if not, the expressions are meaningless. Finally, he attacks the opponents' analogy of spiritual beings taking physical form, calling it empty. He offers the correct view: Gabriel is a subtle being that can become dense and appear visibly, or we speak of annihilation and recreation without physical manifestation. A single substance (jawhar) does not become multiple without addition of other substances, and two extended things cannot interpenetrate, so their analogy is baseless.

أمر
نهي
اقتضاء
طلب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the key difference between a report (khabar) and a command (amr)?
A) A report is about the past, while a command is about the future.
B) A report informs without entailment, while a command entails a request.
C) A report is always true, while a command may be disobeyed.
D) A report is expressed in words, while a command is not.

Key Takeaway
Reports and commands are distinct species of divine speech based on their definitions and content, and cannot be unified into a single type.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read a verse in the Quran, do you immediately recognize whether it is a report (telling you about something) or a command (requiring something from you)? How often do you confuse the two, and what might the consequence be if you treat a command as mere information? Commit today to labeling each verse you read as either 'news' or 'order' before moving on.
?
The author says commands entail a request from God. When you hear a command like 'Establish prayer,' do you sense the urgency of the divine request, or does it become a passive suggestion? What would change in your daily life if you truly heard each command as a personal, immediate demand from your Creator?
?
Reflect on the uniqueness of each report: the story of Yusuf is not the story of Adam. God's speech treats each event and person distinctly. How does this distinctness impact your view of your own life story—do you believe God's speech about your specific circumstances is equally unique and personal? Will you begin reading the Quran as if every report in it is specially addressed to you?

The Genus-Species Unity of Divine Speech
Concession of genus unity, species difference, and refutation of two reductionist views.

Learning Objective
This lesson explains that while reports and commands share the genus of speech, they differ in species, and shows the errors of reducing speech entirely to one type.

Core Idea
Speech is one in genus but diverse in species, like animals and colors.

The author now concedes that reports and commands are united in the general reality of speech (kalamiyya), but they differ in kind, just as animal and human or color and black differ. He then points out the consequences of reducing all speech to either report or command, and refutes those who attribute diversity only to expressions.

Guided Path
1
Understand the genus-species analogy
The author compares speech-ness to animality or color-ness; reports and commands are like human vs animal or black vs color.
This explains how two things can be one in genus but different in species.
2
Note the consequences of reduction
Reducing speech entirely to report nullifies entailment and command; reducing it to command nullifies the meaning of report and cancels stories.
3
Refute expression monism
If expressions correspond to meanings, meanings multiply with expressions; if not, expressions are meaningless. So diversity cannot be reduced to expressions alone.
